||Wahlmotiv: attraktiver<br>Studienort (Index)|Wahlmotiv: Berufschancen am<br>Hochschulort|Vakanzen für hoch qualiﬁzierte<br>am Schulort|**n**|


Should I stay or should I go? Determinanten der räumlichen … 37


weit der Urbanisierungsgrad des Ortes zum Ausdruck, wo die Hochschulreife
erworben wurde. So korreliert die Variable für die mittlere Distanz zu den
nächsten drei Hochschulen mit dem Indikator „Ländlichkeit“ aus der INKARRegionaldatenbank signifikant mit 0,65 (tabellarisch nicht ausgewiesen), da
urbanere Regionen eine größere Hochschuldichte bzw. Nähe zu Hochschulen
haben. [11] Entsprechend lässt sich der genannte Befund auch dahingehend interpretieren, dass die Mobilität aufgrund der regionalen Bildungsinfrastruktur bei
Studienanfänger*innen aus ländlicheren Regionen höher ausfällt als bei denjenigen aus Regionen mit höherem Urbanisierungsgrad. Unter Kontrolle der
Opportunitätsstrukturen am Schulort nimmt der Effekt des Migrationshintergrunds zwischen dem ersten und zweiten Modell zudem deutlich ab und ist
nicht mehr signifikant. Die geringere Mobilität der Studienanfänger*innen mit
Migrationshintergrund hängt demnach damit zusammen, dass Migrant*innen zum
Zeitpunkt des Erwerbs der Hochschulreife häufiger in (urbanen) Regionen mit
einem Hochschulangebot im näheren Umfeld wohnen. Sie müssen für eine Studienaufnahme daher geringere Distanzen zurücklegen als Studienanfänger*innen
ohne Migrationshintergrund, die anteilig stärker in (ländlicheren) Regionen ohne
entsprechende Gelegenheitsstrukturen vertreten sind und daher größere Distanzen bis zur nächsten Hochschule zurücklegen müssen. Ähnlich reduziert sich
auch der (direkte) Effekt der Herkunft aus den ostdeutschen Bundesländern,
wenn das Hochschulangebot am Schulort kontrolliert wird (siehe Modell 1 zu
Modell 2). Studienanfänger*innen aus den ostdeutschen Ländern zeigen entsprechend u. a. aufgrund einer im Vergleich zu Westdeutschland eingeschränkteren
Angebotsstruktur an Studienplätzen eine höhere regionale Mobilität. Im Unterschied zu Migrationshintergrund und regionaler Herkunft nimmt der Effekt des
sozioökonomischen Status unter Kontrolle der Gelegenheitsstrukturen etwas zu.
Studienanfänger*innen mit einem höheren sozioökonomischen Status erwerben
die Hochschulreife zwar häufiger an Schulorten, in deren näherer Umgebung ein
Hochschulangebot vorhanden ist, wählen aber seltener eine der nächstgelegenen
Hochschulen.
Wie in den theoretischen Überlegungen ausgeführt, sollte die regionale Mobilität beim Studienbeginn auch mit den durch die schulische Bildungsbiografie (Art
der Hochschulreife, Schulabschlussnote) gegebenen Handlungsoptionen und den


11 Aufgrund der hohen Korrelation zwischen der Distanz zum Hochschulangebot und dem
Indikator für die Ländlichkeit des Schulortes (Anteil Einwohner*innen auf Kreisebene, die
in Gemeinden mit einer Bevölkerungsdichte von unter 150 Einwohner*innen je km [2] leben)
wurden nicht beide Variablen ins Modell aufgenommen, um Multikollinearitätsprobleme zu
vermeiden.


38 H. Quast et al.


bis zum Zeitpunkt der Mobilitätsentscheidung herausgebildeten personalen Ressourcen (Risikobereitschaft, Big-Five-Persönlichkeitsfaktoren) zusammenhängen.
Diese Merkmale werden in das _dritte Modell_ aufgenommen. Mit Blick auf die
schulische Bildungsbiografie zeigt sich dabei, dass sich Studienanfänger*innen
mit allgemeiner Hochschulreife von allgemeinbildenden und beruflichen Schulen
hinsichtlich der regionalen Mobilität nicht signifikant voneinander unterscheiden. Studienanfänger*innen mit Fachhochschulreife wählen hingegen häufiger
eine (Fach-)Hochschule im näheren Umfeld des Schulortes. Bei ihnen fällt die
regionale Mobilität um 15,54 km signifikant geringer aus als bei Absolvent*innen
allgemeinbildender Schulen mit Abitur. Die schulische Performanz steht ebenfalls
in signifikantem Zusammenhang mit der regionalen Mobilität. So erhöhen um
eine Standardabweichung bessere Schulabschlussnoten die regionale Mobilität
der Studienanfänger*innen um 9,09 km. Erwartungsgemäß sind leistungsstärkere
Schulabsolvent*innen demnach mobiler bei der Hochschulortwahl. Der Zugang
zur Wunschhochschule kann ferner durch Zulassungsbeschränkungen begrenzt
sein. In Modell 3 zeigt sich – unter Kontrolle der Schulabschlussnote –, dass
Schulabsolvent*innen, für die Zulassungsbeschränkungen rückblickend eine größere Rolle bei der Hochschulwahl gespielt haben, eine um 7,51 km signifikant
größere Distanz zur Studienaufnahme zurücklegen. Es liegt nahe, dass diese
Schulabsolvent*innen aufgrund institutioneller Restriktionen der Hochschulen im
näheren Umfeld des Schulortes eine weiter entfernte Hochschule mit niedrigeren
Zulassungsbeschränkungen wählen mussten. Ferner nimmt der direkte Effekt des
sozioökonomischen Status zwischen dem zweiten und dritten Modell etwas ab,
da Studienanfänger*innen aus Familien mit höherem sozioökonomischem Status
im Mittel bessere Schulabschlussnoten erzielen, risikobereiter sind und häufiger eine allgemeine Hochschulreife an allgemeinbildenden Schulen erwerben als
diejenigen mit niedrigerem sozioökonomischen Status.
Regionale Mobilität und damit verbunden der Umzug an einen neuen Lebensort bringen organisatorische und soziale Herausforderungen wie das Einleben
und Zurechtkommen in einem neuen sozialen Umfeld mit sich. Neben den bildungsbiografischen Merkmalen sollten daher bestimmte personale Ressourcen die
Mobilitätsbereitschaft beeinflussen. Wie vermutet, führt eine um eine Standardabweichung höhere Risikobereitschaft dazu, dass sich die regionale Mobilität
signifikant um 5,30 km erhöht, da risikobereite Studienanfänger*innen die Bewältigung der Mobilitätsherausforderungen anscheinend weniger scheuen. Vor dem
Hintergrund des Forschungsstandes wurde des Weiteren erwartet, dass sich insbesondere die Persönlichkeitsmerkmale „Offenheit für neue Erfahrungen“ und
„hohe Geselligkeit“ (Extraversion) aufgrund des mit regionaler Mobilität verbundenen Wechsels des sozialen Umfeldes förderlich auswirken können. Entgegen


Should I stay or should I go? Determinanten der räumlichen … 39


diesen Annahmen stehen diese beiden Persönlichkeitseigenschaften in der vorliegenden Analyse jedoch nicht in signifikantem Zusammenhang mit der räumlichen
Mobilität.
Die Wahl des Hochschulortes hängt nicht nur mit dem bisherigen Bildungsweg, sondern auch mit der Entscheidung für ein bestimmtes Studienfach zusammen. _Im vierten Modell_ werden daher auch die gewählten Studienfächergruppen der Studienanfänger*innen berücksichtigt. Hier zeigt sich,
dass zum einen Studienanfänger*innen der begehrten Fächergruppen Medizin/Gesundheitswissenschaften sowie Rechtswissenschaften deutlich größere
Distanzen zurücklegen als andere. Diese Fächer gehen mit hohen Zulassungsbeschränkungen einher und werden überwiegend bzw. im Falle von Medizin
ausschließlich von Universitäten angeboten. [12] Eine signifikant höhere regionale Mobilität weisen zudem Studienanfänger*innen der vergleichsweise kleinen
Fächergruppe Agrar-/Ernährungs-/Forstwissenschaften auf, da diese Fächer von
einer geringeren Anzahl an Hochschulen angeboten werden.
Im _fünften Modell_ wird abschließend die Bedeutung von Transaktionskosten und Erträgen für die regionale Mobilität in der Spezifikation berücksichtigt.
Wie erwartet und übereinstimmend mit dem bisherigen Forschungsstand (z. B.
Lörz, 2008; Spieß & Wrohlich, 2010), wählen Studienanfänger*innen, für die
nicht-monetäre (soziale) oder finanzielle Mobilitätskosten eine größere Bedeutung
haben, signifikant häufiger Hochschulen im näheren Umfeld ihres Schulortes und
legen entsprechend geringere Distanzen zurück. Bezüglich des quantitativen Ausmaßes der Distanz mindert insbesondere eine hohe Bedeutung nicht-monetärer
Kosten die Entfernung zwischen dem Heimatort und der Hochschule bei der Studienaufnahme (AME = -37,50). Unter Berücksichtigung der Kosten und Erträge
reduziert sich zudem der direkte Effekt des sozioökonomischen Status deutlich und ist nicht mehr signifikant (siehe Modell 4 zu Modell 5). Zusätzliche,
hier nicht ausgewiesene Modellschritte, in denen die Kosten und Erträge einzeln
aufgenommen werden, zeigen, dass dies insbesondere mit einer herkunftsspezifischen Bedeutung der Mobilitätskosten zusammenhängt. Studienanfänger*innen
mit einem höheren sozioökonomischen Status bewerten die Mobilitätskosten
geringer und legen daher auch größere Distanzen bei der Hochschulwahl zurück
(vgl. hierzu z. B. auch Lörz, 2008).
Mit Blick auf die Ertragsüberlegungen im fünften Modell erscheint es zunächst
etwas überraschend, dass lediglich hochschulexterne Faktoren, die sich auf
eine hedonistische Orientierung während der Studienzeit beziehen, die regionale


12 Unter Berücksichtigung der Studienfächer reduziert sich auch der direkte Effekt der
Bedeutung von Zulassungsbeschränkungen zwischen dem dritten und vierten Modell.


40 H. Quast et al.


Mobilität signifikant erhöhen. So legen Studienanfänger*innen, für die ein attraktiver Studienort mit guter Atmosphäre und hohem Freizeitangebot von Bedeutung
ist, zur Studienaufnahme größere Distanzen zurück (AME = 4,86). Demgegenüber zeigt sich hier weder für längerfristige Ertragserwartungen, die sich auf
gute Berufschancen nach dem Studium am Hochschulort beziehen, noch für
kurzfristigere Ertragsmotive, die sich auf das Studium an sich beziehen (hohe
Studienqualität, Passung mit dem Fachinteresse), ein signifikanter Zusammenhang mit der regionalen Mobilität. Das heißt aber nicht, dass diese Motive für
die Hochschulwahl generell nicht von Bedeutung sind. Wie eingangs skizziert
wurde, sind insbesondere die Passung zwischen Studienangebot und fachlichem
Interesse sowie eine gute Studienqualität für nahezu alle Studienanfänger*innen
wichtige Motive der Hochschulwahl. Vielmehr scheinen diese ertragsbezogenen
Hochschulwahlmotive gleichermaßen für diejenigen, die eine Hochschule in der
Nähe des Schulortes wählen, als auch für diejenigen, die größere Distanzen
zwischen Schul- und Hochschulort zurücklegen, von Bedeutung zu sein.


**5** **Zusammenfassung und Ausblick**


Die regionale Mobilität zwischen dem Ort des Erwerbs der Hochschulreife und
dem Hochschulort ist zum einen für die Mikroebene der Studienanfänger*innen
von Bedeutung, zum anderen spielt sie auch für regionale Arbeitsmarktstrukturen
oder Kapazitätsplanungen der Hochschulen eine Rolle. Vor diesem Hintergrund
ist dieser Beitrag der Frage nachgegangen, welche Determinanten die geografische Mobilität bei der Studienaufnahme bedingen. Im Unterschied zu anderen
Studien, die Mobilität beispielsweise als Bundeslandwechsel oder dichotom als
das Verlassen eines bestimmten Umkreises betrachten (z. B. Weßling & Bechler, 2019), wurde hier als Mobilitätsmaß die geografische Distanz zwischen der
Schul- und der Hochschuladresse in Kilometern verwendet.
In den theoretischen Überlegungen wurden potentielle Determinanten der
Mobilitätsentscheidung von Studienanfänger*innen in Anlehnung an verschiedene theoretische Ansätze diskutiert und identifiziert. Vor dem Hintergrund
werterwartungstheoretischer Überlegungen wurde angenommen, dass höhere
antizipierte Mobilitätserträge die geografische Mobilität erhöhen, wohingegen
hohe erwartete Mobilitätskosten diese mindern sollten. Neben soziodemografischen Merkmalen, Gelegenheitsstrukturen und vorgelagerten Bildungspfaden
(Lebensverlaufsperspektive) wurden in Anlehnung an entwicklungspsychologische Ansätze auch personale Ressourcen in die Überlegungen einbezogen.


Should I stay or should I go? Determinanten der räumlichen … 41


Die empirischen Ergebnisse haben gezeigt, dass geografische Mobilität mit der
hochschulischen Opportunitätsstruktur zusammenhängt. Studienanfänger*innen
bleiben häufiger im näheren Umfeld des Ortes, wo sie ihre Hochschulreife
erworben haben, wenn hier auch ein Hochschulangebot vorhanden ist. Unter Kontrolle dieser Gelegenheitsstrukturen erhöhen bessere Schulabschlussleistungen,
eine höhere Risikobereitschaft und der Schulabschluss einer allgemeinbildenden Schule – wie erwartet – die Mobilität signifikant. In Anlehnung an den
Forschungsstand (Zimmermann & Neyer, 2013) wurde erwartet, dass sich die
Persönlichkeitsmerkmale Offenheit für neue Erfahrungen und ein hohes Maß an
Extraversion förderlich auf die Mobilität auswirken, da mit regionaler Mobilität
ein Wechsel des sozialen Umfelds verbunden sein kann. Jedoch hat sich in der
empirischen Analyse kein signifikanter Zusammenhang zwischen den prospektiv
erhobenen Big-Five-Persönlichkeitsmerkmalen und der Mobilität gezeigt.
Mit Blick auf Mobilitätskosten hat die vorliegende Analyse ebenso wie schon
vorherige Studien (Lörz, 2008) ergeben, dass höhere antizipierte monetäre und
nicht-monetäre Kostenerwartungen die Mobilität bei der Studienaufnahme signifikant mindern. Ein bezüglich der Entfernung sehr deutlicher Effekt hat sich dabei
für die nicht-monetären Kosten gezeigt, die durch das Verlassen des gewohnten sozialen Umfeldes entstehen können. Hinsichtlich der ertragsbezogenen
Hochschulwahlmotive präferieren Studienanfänger*innen, für die ein attraktiver
Studienort mit guter Atmosphäre und hohem Freizeitangebot von Bedeutung ist,
zwar erwartungsgemäß häufiger auch weiter entfernte Hochschulen. Für weitere
ertragsbezogene Hochschulwahlmotive (wie etwa eine höhere Passung zwischen
fachlichen Interessen und Hochschulangebot, eine höhere Studienqualität und
gute Beschäftigungschancen am Studienort nach dem Studium) hat sich entgegen den Erwartungen jedoch kein signifikant positiver Zusammenhang mit der
Mobilität gezeigt. Dieser Befund lässt sich teils durch die mangelnde Varianz
dieser ertragsbezogenen Hochschulwahlmotive bei mobilen und weniger mobilen
Studienanfänger*innen erklären. So scheinen diese Motive für die Wahl einer
Hochschule im näheren Umfeld wie auch für die Wahl einer weiter entfernten Hochschule ähnlich bedeutsam zu sein. Denkbar wäre zudem, dass diese
ertragsbezogenen Motive erst bei der späteren Wahl einer Hochschule für das
Masterstudium stärker ins Gewicht fallen (vgl. aber Ohlendorf et al., 2022 in
diesem Band) und weniger beim ersten Übergang ins Studium.
Während sich zwischen dem Alter beim Studienbeginn und der Mobilität kein
signifikanter Zusammenhang gezeigt hat, haben die Analysen entsprechend der
theoretischen Erwartung und dem Forschungsstand (z. B. Lörz, 2008) einen signifikanten Effekt des sozioökonomischen Status auf die räumliche Distanz zwischen


42 H. Quast et al.


Schulort und gewähltem Studienort ergeben. Je höher der sozioökonomische Status des Elternhauses ist, desto größere Distanzen werden zurückgelegt. In den
vorliegenden Analysen wird die größere Mobilität der Studienanfänger*innen aus
statushöheren Sozialgruppen u. a. darüber vermittelt, dass sie häufiger eine allgemeine Hochschulreife an einer allgemeinbildenden Schule erwerben sowie im
Mittel bessere Schulabschlussnoten und eine höhere Risikobereitschaft aufweisen als diejenigen aus statusniedrigeren Sozialgruppen. Darüber hinaus spielen
Mobilitätskosten bei ihrer Hochschulwahl eine geringere Rolle.
Die verwendeten Daten haben den Vorteil, dass sie die Integration zahlreicher Determinanten in einem Analysemodell zu regionaler Mobilität erlauben.
Neben Informationen zur Soziodemografie und zu vorgelagerten Bildungspfaden sind Persönlichkeitsmerkmale sowie subjektive Einschätzungen zu Kosten
und Erträgen der Hochschulortwahl in den Daten enthalten. Diese wurden aufgrund des Längsschnittcharakters noch vor der Entscheidung für eine Hochschule
erhoben, wodurch eine rückwirkende Rationalisierung subjektiver Einschätzungen vermieden wird. Im Unterschied zu anderen Studien konnte dadurch zudem
die subjektive Bedeutung von Mobilitätskosten und -erträgen direkt abgebildet werden. Es musste hierfür nicht auf regionale Kontextmerkmale auf der
Makroebene (z. B. Beschäftigungsquote, Mietpreise) als Proxy für individuelle Ertrags- und Kostenüberlegungen zurückgegriffen werden. Demgegenüber
konnte mit den verwendeten Daten jedoch nicht geklärt werden, inwieweit
die Mobilität zwischen Schul- und Hochschulort mit einem Wohnortwechsel
einhergeht oder ob es sich vielmehr um reine Pendelmobilität handelt. Diese
Unterscheidung könnte beispielsweise deshalb relevant sein, weil soziale Kosten bei Pendelmobilität geringer ausfallen können. Zukünftige Analysen sollten
diese Unterscheidung der Mobilitätsformen berücksichtigen. Eine weitere Limitation liegt darin, dass für die vorliegende Untersuchung nur die erste und zweite
Welle des DZHW-Studienberechtigtenpanels vorlagen. Da sich etwa ein Drittel
der Studienberechtigten zum Zeitpunkt der zweiten Welle (ein halbes Jahr nach
Schulabschluss) noch in Übergangstätigkeiten wie einem Freiwilligen Sozialen
Jahr oder einem Auslandsaufenthalt befand und noch nicht an eine Hochschule
übergegangen ist, konnten diese nicht in die Analyse einbezogen werden. Es liegt
jedoch nahe, dass sich gerade Übergangstätigkeiten, die bereits mit räumlicher
Mobilität bzw. einem Wohnortwechsel verbunden sind, vorteilhaft auf die spätere
Mobilität bei der Hochschulwahl auswirken könnten. In diesem Falle könnte das
Ausmaß der regionalen Mobilität beim Studienbeginn in der vorliegenden Studie unterschätzt werden. Hier könnten Analysen mit späteren Befragungswellen
anknüpfen.

##### **Going on for the master’s degree.** **Does the university make a difference** **for graduates’ decisions?**

Susanne Falk, Christina Klug and Maike Reimer


~~**Abstract**~~


After the degree structure of German universities changed from a one-cycle
to a two-cycle system with bachelor’s and master’s degrees, bachelor graduates’ options have been (1) entry into the labour market, (2) enrolment in a
master’s programme at the same or (3) some other university. While previous
studies concentrate on individual-level or regional context factors, we focus on
universities’ institutional characteristics and their impact on graduates’ decisions whether and where to continue higher education. Using multilevel models
and data from a cohort of bachelor graduates in Bavaria (Germany), we analyse the impact of individual- and university-level characteristics separately
for four fields of study. In general, mobility between bachelor and master
is not particularly common, even within Germany. While certain effects are
found in all four fields of study, there are also field-specific effects. Among
the university characteristics, university type has the most pervasive impact.
However, graduates’ perception of the quality of education and tutoring can


S. Falk · M. Reimer ( ~~B~~ )
Bavarian State Institute for Higher Education Research and Planning (IHF), Munich,
Germany
[E-Mail: reimer@ihf.bayern.de](mailto:reimer@ihf.bayern.de)


S. Falk
[E-Mail: falk@ihf.bayern.de](mailto:falk@ihf.bayern.de)


C. Klug
German Youth Institute (DJI), Munich, Germany
[E-Mail: klug@dji.de](mailto:klug@dji.de)



© Der/die Autor(en), exklusiv lizenziert durch Springer Fachmedien
Wiesbaden GmbH, ein Teil von Springer Nature 2022
M. Jungbauer-Gans und A. Gottburgsen (Hrsg.), _Regionale Mobilität und_
_Hochschulbildung,_ Higher Education Research und Science Studies,
[https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-658-36156-3_3](https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-658-36156-3_3)



47


48 S. Falk et al.


influence their decision, as well as the local research orientation and master
opportunities.


~~**Keywords**~~


Higher education [•] Bologna Process [•] Multilevel analysis [•] Transition [•]
Mobility


**1** **Introduction**


At the turn of the twenty-first century, European ministers in charge of higher
education agreed to implement far-reaching reforms in their countries: The socalled “Bologna Process” sought to harmonize higher education systems by
(among other things) implementing a two-cycle structure of degrees in more than
40 participating countries. In Germany, bachelor’s and master’s degrees replaced the former one-cycle system that had led all students to a level more or
less equivalent to a master’s degree. Social mobility research has investigated
graduate behaviour at the new transition from bachelor to master to determine if
the explicit goal of the Bologna Process to enable children from non-academic
backgrounds to successfully participate in higher education has been reached (e.g.
Auspurg & Hinz, 2011; Lörz et al., 2019; Neugebauer et al., 2016). Fewer studies
address a change of institution at the transition into a master’s degree programme
(Kaiser & Rudel, 2018; Ohlendorf et al., 2022, in this volume), although the
promotion of mobility between institutions was another prominent goal of the
Bologna Reform. For students, a change in university when starting a master’s
programme could result in more suitable employment and, prospectively, higher
wages upon entering the labour market (Kaiser & Rudel, 2018). For universities
and regions, it is important to attract or retain master’s students, as many of them
remain in the region after graduation, thus compensating for a local shortage of
skilled workers (Falk & Kratz, 2009; Krabel & Flöther, 2014), and the human
capital of locally employed graduates is an important growth factor for regions
(Kratz & Lenz, 2015).
Even so, the institutional context of individual higher education (e.g. opportunities for further research-focused studies or the study experience itself) and its
role in shaping the bachelor-master-transition have been less widely researched
in Germany than in other higher education systems such as the US system (e.g.
English & Umbach, 2016; Mullen et al., 2003). Recent studies in Germany focus
on the mobility between university types at the bachelor-master threshold (Lörz
et al., 2019; Roloff, 2019) and examine individual factors that may influence


Going on for the master’s degree. Does the university make a … 49


the post-bachelor transition, such as age, gender, academic performance, and
parental educational background. Another line of research analyses the impact of
contextual factors associated with the universities where bachelor’s and master’s
degrees were obtained and their location (such as labour market and demographic
characteristics, Kaiser & Rudel, 2018; Ohlendorf et al., 2022, in this volume).
In this paper, we include relevant individual characteristics, but focus on the
university-level characteristics of the bachelor university to address the following
questions:


1. Which characteristics associated with a university influence graduates’ decision to pursue a master’s degree?
2. Which characteristics associated with a university influence graduates’ decision to take up master’s studies at their bachelor university or switch to another
institution?


Using a representative sample from a cohort of bachelor graduates from 26
universities in Bavaria (Germany), we employ multilevel modelling to analyse
the individual decisions occurring in particular educational contexts. In Sect. 2,
we develop an analytical framework that includes both individual-level and
university-level factors and takes into account the institutional and organisational
structure of the German higher education system. We review the existing literature and formulate hypotheses. Sect. 3 describes the data, statistical methods and
descriptive results. The results of the multilevel analyses are presented in Sect. 4,
and their implications and limitations are discussed in Sect. 5.


**2** **Analytical framework**


**2.1** **General outline**


From the perspective of subjective expected utility theory, the decision to pursue
a master’s degree is the result of a rational deliberation in which the expected
returns to a master’s degree are valued more favourably than the expected costs
(Boudon, 1974; Breen & Goldthorpe, 1997). Individuals base their investment
decision on weighing potential benefits, anticipated material and immaterial


50 S. Falk et al.


costs [1] and subjective likelihood of success (Breen & Goldthorpe, 1997; English & Umbach, 2016; Perna, 2006). Enrolling in a master’s programme may
bring material and immaterial returns in the future but increases present costs
and entails the risk of failure. The transition to another university may enhance
future benefits if the master’s programmes available there match the individual’s
career development prospects better or if admission requirements at the former university can’t be met, but it may also increase costs or risks due to the
lesser familiarity with the new university (Scheller et al., 2013). Moreover, increased commuting distance or the need to relocate may incur additional costs that
could deter graduates from switching to another university and ultimately from
taking up a master’s programme altogether. The deliberation process is influenced by factors on multiple levels that are nested within each other (English &
Umbach, 2016): Educational organisations such as universities provide settings in
which individual perceptions and aspirations for further education and careers are
shaped. They frame students’ aspirations and decisions by offering a particular
view of and preparation for the expected educational and professional path after
graduation. The “institutional habitus” (Smyth & Hannan, 2007) of an educational
organisation is embodied in (among other things) teaching styles, values and
beliefs of the teaching staff as well as in the curriculum.


**2.2** **Individual-level factors influencing transition decisions**


At the individual level, several interrelated factors affect the decision whether
to enter the labour market or a master’s degree programme (for an overview of
hypotheses, see Table 1). Studies on bachelor graduates from different _parental_
_educational backgrounds_ show clearly that in Germany as well as in the United
States, the United Kingdom (UK) and Austria, enrolment in a master’s programme is more likely for graduates from academic families (Auspurg & Hinz,
2011; Ellwood & Kane, 2000; English & Umbach, 2016; Gärtner & Himpele,
2010; Giani, 2015; Lörz et al., 2019; Mullen et al., 2003; Neugebauer et al.,
2016; Purcell et al., 2012). [2] Also, individuals with better _previous school and_


1 In Germany, higher education is tax-funded and most study programmes are noncontributory. Therefore, material costs assume the form of going without income for a longer
period of time or of lost income during studies.
2 This is in line with status reproduction theory, which argues that the driving aspiration in
educational decision-making is to reach at least the same educational level as one’s parents.
If a parent has an academic degree, it will be a (pre-Bologna) one-cycle degree, so that the
student’s subjective aspirational level should be above a bachelor’s degree.


Going on for the master’s degree. Does the university make a … 51

_university performance_ are more likely to pursue a master’s degree in Germany,
the US and the UK, as they can expect to be successful at the master level (Ellwood & Kane, 2000; English & Umbach, 2016; Lörz et al., 2019; Mullen et al.,
2003; Neugebauer et al., 2016). _Gender_ makes a difference in some national and
international studies, in that female graduates slightly more often take up a master’s degree in Germany (Auspurg & Hinz, 2011; Lörz et al., 2019). This seems
to be partly due to gender-specific choices of subject and university type. Factors
associated with personal circumstances, such as _older age,_ reduce the likelihood
to take up a master’s programme because the expected economic benefits of this
educational investment decline with age (Auspurg & Hinz, 2011; Lörz et al.,
2019).
_Differential educational pathways_ before entering higher education are linked
to differential experiences with learning opportunities and educational options.
The German secondary school system offers different tracks and different certificates that permit entrance either to universities in general _(Abitur)_ or to
universities of applied sciences only _(Fachhochschulreife)._ It has been shown
that secondary school graduates with _Abitur_ are more likely to take up a master’s
degree (Lörz et al., 2015, 2019; Neugebauer et al., 2016). This effect is due to
a large extent, but not entirely, to the fact that graduates with _Abitur_ more often
study at a university rather than a university of applied sciences.
Another dimension of the educational pathway – in this case after deciding
to enter higher education – is the choice of an _academic field._ Different fields of
study attract different kinds of students and offer different socializing environments. Moreover, the professional options for those who enter the labour market
with the bachelor’s degree are not equally attractive across disciplines in Germany and other European countries (Bittmann, 2019; van de Werfhorst, 2008).
The relative superiority of the master’s degree over the bachelor’s and the time
it takes for the additional degree to pay off also varies between disciplines (Neugebauer & Weiss, 2018; Trennt, 2019; Wieschke et al., 2020). It is therefore not


Going on for the master’s degree. Does the university make a … 53


surprising that master’s degrees are much more common in some fields of study
(namely humanities, maths and natural sciences) than in others (management,
computer engineering or social work; Fabian et al., 2016).
In addition, German studies revealed that the _degree of integration into the_
_(local) university,_ into academic activities and networks can influence a graduate’s
decision-making process by strengthening the institutional habitus. Lörz et al.
(2019) found that graduates who reported better contacts with peers and teachers
were more likely to take up a master’s degree; Rehn et al. (2011) showed the
same for students holding a student job. Also, a _foreign study experience,_ i.e.
time spent abroad for internships or exchange programmes, has been shown to
increase the likelihood of a master’s degree as the respective students tend to have
a privileged parental background, better grades and stronger career ambitions
(Neugebauer et al., 2016; Rehn et al., 2011).
Beyond objective factors, _individual perceptions and experiences of the uni-_
_versity education_ play an important role for educational decisions. In the US,
perceived quality of the curriculum is among the most important predictors of
college enrolment (Perna, 2006) and _indicators of good teaching practices_ influence aspirations to go to graduate school (Hanson et al., 2016). For transitions
from secondary to tertiary education in the UK, Reay et al. (2005) found that
school guidance quality and curriculum offers are the central dimensions that
influence educational aspirations and transitions. Similarly, it has been shown for
Germany that a _positive experience of studying_ enhances the probability of aspiring to continue at the master’s level and actually doing so. More specifically, a
master was more likely if graduates evaluated the university’s research focus and
infrastructure positively (Lörz et al., 2019) or expressed general satisfaction with
their study programme (Kretschmann et al., 2017).
The patterns and determinants of _changing to another institution_ have not been
researched as thoroughly. Most of the German bachelor graduates who take up
a master’s degree stay at their bachelor university (Fabian et al., 2016; Scheller et al., 2013). This trend is most pronounced in science and engineering and
reflects the preference of graduates for their home university. For the transition
from school to higher education, it has been shown that _school grades_ and _social_
_origin_ influence the probability of _changing one’s residence_ since lower social origin is often associated with more precarious financing and higher cost-sensitivity,
and lower school grades with higher risks of failure (Helbig et al., 2017). Studies
on the change of university at any time during studies, while including largely the
pre-Bologna one-cycle degrees, find a higher propensity to change for _women,_
students with an _academic family background_ and the _Abitur_ (Krawietz, 2008;
Lörz, 2008).


54 S. Falk et al.


While _academic and social integration_ during bachelor’s studies can increase
the inclination to continue towards a master’s degree, they may simultaneously
decrease the likelihood of doing so at some other university, since costs increase when local ties and connections have to be severed (Mullen et al., 2003).
Having spent _time abroad during studies_ could indicate a higher inclination
towards mobility and thus make a change of university more likely. Focusing
on bachelor graduates from universities of applied sciences who take up a master’s programme at a university (which necessitates a change of institution), Lörz
and Neugebauer (2019) found that such a transition is more likely for students
of maths and natural sciences, for younger students, for those with an academic
family background and for those with better academic performance. At the same
time, a _positive evaluation_ of research conditions at the bachelor institution makes
it more likely to continue at a university of applied sciences (the same or another
one).


**2.3** **University-level characteristics influencing transition**
**decisions**


The most obvious and pervasive organisational differences result from the binary
structure of the German higher education system. The two _university types_
(research university vs. university of applied sciences) attract different kinds of
students and create different educational milieus (Kramer et al., 2012). Research
universities are equally focused on basic research and teaching; their educational
objective is to promote academic and scientific training. They offer the more
prestigious and financially rewarding study programmes and can award doctoral
degrees (Hüther & Krücken, 2018; Müller & Wolbers, 2003; Witte et al., 2008).
Universities of applied sciences focus on teaching, mainly in business, engineering/informatics and social fields, and chiefly conduct applied research. Their
curricula are tailored to needs of the labour market and include prolonged practical experience. Their teaching staff is selected according to their experience
with applying scientific knowledge to professional fields outside academia. Consequently, the majority of master’s degree programmes is offered at universities
(HRK, 2016), and graduates from universities of applied sciences more often
change to a research university for their master’s degree than the other way round
(Fabian et al., 2016).
While the distinction between research universities and universities of applied
sciences is well researched, differences between organisations of the same type
have been explicitly discussed only recently, and research is still scarce. One


Going on for the master’s degree. Does the university make a … 55


aspect that may be relevant is universities’ _focus on research_ and consequently,
orientation of students towards master level degrees. On the one hand, attempts are made to distinguish between _more and less elite research universities_
(Hüther & Krücken, 2018). On the other hand, a “blurring of boundaries” or “academic drift” has been observed (Witte et al., 2008) as representatives of the more
research-oriented universities of applied sciences demand a recognition of their
contribution to and potential for applied as well as increasingly also basic research. [3] In the US and Hungary, more research-oriented universities have exhibited
a higher transition rate into master’s programmes (English & Umbach, 2016;
Nyüsti & Veroszta, 2015). With respect to a change of institution, a low research
orientation at the bachelor-awarding university could motivate students to move
to another institution for their master’s degree (Lörz & Neugebauer, 2019).
Since both relocation and change of institution are costly in their own way,
the _opportunities_ that present themselves at graduates’ home universities may
be relevant for both decisions. Lörz et al. (2019) found that a local offer of a
master’s programme in a graduate’s field of study makes the transition into a
master’s programme at the same university more likely. [4] Conversely, master’s
students who had changed universities gave as their most frequent reason that
their desired master’s programme was not offered at their home university (Fabian
et al., 2016; Scheller et al., 2013).
Both the sheer _number_ of students a university can admit to master’s programmes and the probability of finding a _suitable_ master’s programme can be
relevant. Moreover, a larger or more varied offer may be indicative of a greater
degree of research orientation at the university in question. The local opportunity
structure for students, however, is not necessarily restricted to their home university. There is evidence for the US (Turley, 2009), Canada (Frenette, 2006) and
Germany (Helbig et al., 2017) that the _distance to the closest university_ affects
school leavers’ decision to enrol in higher education in the first place. Also,
changes of university at any point during studies were found to occur more often
if another university was close by (Lörz, 2008). Correspondingly, the likelihood
of changing to another university may increase if a university is located in the
proximity, which allows for switching without actually relocating.


3 Such efforts have been successful: In several federal states in Germany, universities of
applied sciences can apply for the right to award doctoral degrees if they demonstrate sufficient research strength in a given field or area.
4 For Hungary, however, Nyüsti and Veroszta (2015) find no such connection.


56 S. Falk et al.


**3** **Method**


**3.1** **Data and analysis strategy**


We use data from the Bavarian Graduate Panel (BGP), a representative panel
study of higher education graduates in Bavaria (Germany), which covers 9 out of
10 research universities [5] and all 17 state-funded universities of applied sciences.
We examine data from the 2013/2014 graduation cohort that was surveyed about
1.5 years after graduation. The response rate was 32.9 % (Falk et al., 2018). The
graduates reported comprehensive information about their educational biography
before and after graduation. After excluding graduates with other degrees, and
graduates from smaller fields of study only offered at few institutions (such as
art and sports), the data set contains 8,355 individuals with a bachelor’s degree
who either took up a master’s programme afterwards or did not. The number of
graduates surveyed per university ranges from 62 to 937.
Information at institutional level was gathered from two sources. First, we
obtained information about the bachelor’s and master’s study programmes directly
from the universities. [6] Second, we retrieved information about study opportunities
and indicators of research orientation for each university through an information system (ICEland) which contains official records of German universities
and is maintained by the German Centre for Higher Education Research and
Science Studies (Deutsches Zentrum für Hochschul- und Wissenschaftsforschung,
DZHW): number of students in bachelor’s and master’s programmes and number
of third-party funded staff. Because of the hierarchical data structure, we applied
a logistic multilevel analysis, running one model for each of the four larger fields
of study. The estimates result in a coefficient and a random error at each level
(Georg, 2009) for each field of study:


Individual level: _yij_ = _β_ 0 _j_ + _rij_ (1)


Aggregatelevel: _β_ 0 _j_ = _γ_ 00 + _u_ 0 _j_ (2)


Here, _yij_ depicts the measured value of individual _i_ in group _j,_ and _β0j_ is the
intercept of each group, which consists at the aggregate level of the overall sample


5 One research university chose not to participate in the Bavarian Graduate Panel.
6 We are very grateful to our collaborators at all participating universities and universities of
applied sciences for providing complete lists.


Going on for the master’s degree. Does the university make a … 57


mean _γ 00_ and the random error _u0j._ The following random intercept model is
applied:


Individual level:


_yij_ = _β_ 0 _j_ + _β_ 1 _(gender)_ + _β_ 2 _(age)_ + _. . ._ + _rij_ (3)


Aggregate level:


_β_ 0 _j_ = _γ_ 00 + _γ_ 01 _(type of higher education institution)_ + _. . ._ + _γ_ 07 _(diversity)_ + _u_ 0 _j_
(4)


Finally, we computed logistic regression analyses for both higher education
institutions on the transition to a master’s degree in a comprehensive model and
estimated the average marginal effects (AME) of the results, thus uncovering the
effects of individual-level and university-level variables on the transition decision.
Due to the fundamental, multi-layered and deeply entrenched differences between
fields of studies that reflect disciplinary and labour market “cultures” and their
interplays, we model the effects separately for larger disciplinary groups or fields
of study.
For the first series of field-specific models, we include all bachelor graduates.
The dependent variable is binary, with 1 indicating that the individual has taken
up a master’s programme within the observation period and 0 that they have not
(yet) done so. In the second series of field-specific models, we focus on those
bachelor graduates who have entered a master’s programme. Here, the dependent
variable is again binary and assumes the value of 0 if the study programme is
located at the same institution as the bachelor’s degree, and 1 if the individual
has switched to some other university.


**3.2** **Operationalization**


_Individual-level variables:_ For _parental educational background,_ we include a
dummy variable that is 1 if at least one parent has a higher education degree
(reference category 0: no parent with academic background). _School performance_
is measured by the final grade of the graduation certificate. For _educational_
_pathway_ to higher education, we include the kind of higher education entrance
qualification as dummy ( _Abitur_ versus other higher education entrance qualification as reference category). _Gender_ is included as male (1), with female (0) as


58 S. Falk et al.


reference category, and _age_ as a metric variable. _Study performance_ is operationalized as the final grade of the bachelor’s degree, but due to different grading
cultures in different fields of study (e.g. Müller-Benedict & Grözinger, 2017),
all grades are z-standardized at the discipline-specific mean, and displayed as
individual deviations up- or downwards. Graduates’ _integration into the (local)_
_university environment_ enters into the models as presence of a study-related or
other job (reference: no job at all) and internships (reference: no internships), followed by study-related stays abroad (1 when present, 0 when absent as reference
category).
We include two indicators of how _positive the study experience_ was perceived
during bachelor studies: first, students’ subjective rating of study quality, calculated as an index of ratings of several items [7] on a 5-point scale ranging from 1
(very good) to 5 (very bad), and second, their evaluation of subject-related support and tutoring quality measured with the same scale, converted into a dummy
variable: 1 if respondents answered with 1 or 2 and 0 if they chose 3, 4 or 5
(reference category).
_University-level variables:_ University type is included, with the value 1 for
research university and 0 for university of applied sciences (reference category).
To capture differences between individual institutions independent of the type
of university, we developed a measure of _research strength_ that relies on staff
structure. We calculated the proportion of staff funded by third parties (usually via
research grants) at the bachelor-awarding university in relation to the proportion
of staff financed via basic funding. This measure ranges from 0 (no third-party
funded members of staff) to 0.4 (40 % of staff funded by third-party grants).
For the _opportunity structure_ to pursue a master’s degree at the home university, we include two independent indicators that address two dimensions of
the master’s programmes offered. The first measure aims to capture the range of
choices available. Detailed lists provided by all participating universities allow
for computing the ratio of master’s to bachelor’s programmes within each field
of study. Overall, the higher education institutions reported 667 bachelor’s programmes and 681 master’s programmes. The indicator stands for the field-specific


7 These items are: access to courses (available places); match of course content with examination requirements; scheduling of courses; coordination of the contents of courses/modules;
transparency of course content, requirements and organisation; appropriate distribution of
workload; alignment with (current) practical requirements; alignment with (current) research
questions; opportunities for specialization; linkage of theory and practice.


Going on for the master’s degree. Does the university make a … 59


_diversity_ of the master opportunity structure, [8] with values ranging from 0 (no
master’s programmes offered in a given field at a university) to 4.9 (almost five
master’s programmes for each bachelor’s). This indicator was grouped in three
categories: fewer master’s than bachelor’s programmes at home university, equal
number of bachelor’s and master’s programmes (reference category) and more
master’s than bachelor’s programmes.
The second measure targets the sheer field-specific _capacity_ at master level.
We calculated the ratio of master’s students to bachelor’s students for each field
of study at each university. This indicator does not accurately reflect the ratio of
maximum available master openings to maximum available bachelor openings,
since programmes at both levels may include fewer students than theoretically
possible. But as there is no more precise measure for the number of students a
university can accommodate on the levels of bachelor’s or master’s degree, we
consider our ratio as a rough and useful approximation. In those cases where there
were only bachelor’s but no master’s programmes, or vice versa, we replaced the
ratio with 0. Therefore, capacities range from 0 to 0.9 (the latter indicating that
there is almost the same number of students at master level as at bachelor level).
In the model with the transition rate into another university as dependent
variable, we furthermore include the distance to the next university, assuming
that there is a threshold effect (Helbig et al., 2017): distances below a certain
value constitute no obstacle to changing universities, while all distances above
the threshold make relocating necessary. In accordance with previous studies
(e.g. Hüning et al., 2017), the threshold is set at a distance of 50 km between
universities, since the average distance of a postal code district to the nearest
university location as the crow flies is 47 km in Bavaria. To more realistically
capture commuting costs, we rely on the number of kilometres by car between
the home university and those two other universities that are closest (determined
with Google Maps [TM] ) and we generate a dummy variable that is 0 if the two
closest universities are farther away than 50 km and 1 if they are closer.
For field of study we use the classification by the Federal Ministry of Statistics
(Statistisches Bundesamt), which has the advantage of yielding sufficiently large
groups for quantitative models and is also incorporated into the ICEland information system. In our data, we can work with four fields of study (language and
cultural studies; law, economics and social sciences; maths and natural sciences;
engineering).


8 Some interdisciplinary master’s programmes are open to graduates from more than one
field (e.g., “Cognitive Neurosciences” admits graduates from biology, psychology and medicine).


60 S. Falk et al.


**3.3** **Descriptive results**


Figure 1 shows transition rates to a master’s programme at the same university
(darker part of the bar) or another university (lighter part). [9] The percentage of
graduates taking up a master’s programme is 69.2 % across the whole sample
but varies from 35.5 to 96.6 percent. The percentage of graduates who remain
at the home university for the master’s programme ranges from 6.5 to 88.1; the
percentage of switchers varies from 8.6 to 53.9 % of all graduates (or from 8.9
to 95.7 % of all graduates taking up a master’s programme).
There is a clear pattern for the transition rates: graduates of research universities (marked by dotted bars) are much more likely to go on for a master’s
degree. For mobility, the pattern is more mixed, but the institutions with the
lowest percentage of switchers are again research universities. [10] Otherwise we
observe substantial heterogeneity within each university type. While this is partly
due to different disciplinary structures, transition rates and the probability of
switching also differ within fields of study (results available on request).
Table 2 gives an overview of all variables and their distribution, separately for
each field of study, within the two groups of the first dependent variable. We see
that in two fields of study, the percentage of _men_ who take up a master’s degree
is higher (language and cultural studies; law, economics and social sciences),
while in the other two (maths, natural sciences; engineering), it is the other way
round and the percentage of women is higher. Those who take up a master’s
programme are on average _younger_ and more often have at least one _academic_
_parent_ and _Abitur._ This group also _evaluates study quality and tutoring of their_
_bachelor university more positively_ and has more often spent _time abroad. Jobs_
and _internships during bachelor studies_ are more widespread among those who
take up a master’s degree in some fields of study, but not in maths and natural
sciences.
Among the _university-level variables, university type_ again shows a marked
influence, with graduates from research universities being more likely to continue.
Also, the proportion of _third-party funded staff_ is higher for those who continue.
The _diversity_ of master’s programmes does not show any clear pattern, whereas


9 Together with those graduates who did not take up master level studies, mainly to enter the
labour market, each bar would add up to 100 percent of graduates.
10 As universities of applied sciences offer fewer master’s programmes, their graduates frequently change to a research university in order to pursue a master’s degree. Such transitions
make up 29 percent of all changes of institution. The percentage is lower (1 percent) in law,
economics and social studies, equal (32 percent) in maths and natural sciences, and higher
(51 percent) in engineering.

the sheer _capacity_ in the respective field of study is positively associated with the
inclination to enter a master’s programme.
In Table 3, the values are shown for the second dependent variable. _Women_
make up a higher proportion of those who become mobile, in all fields of study,
when taking up a master’s programme, while _age_ does not make any difference.
Graduates from _academic families_ and with _Abitur_ switch more often. Graduates in all fields of study who remain at their home university appear to have
better _school grades,_ while for bachelor grades this only applies in engineering.
_Study-related jobs_ and _internships_ are not consistently associated with transitions
to other universities, but those graduates who switch more often have _foreign_
_study experience. University_ graduates are overrepresented among switchers, and
a lower _research orientation of the bachelor university_ is associated with higher
rates of change to another university. Also, those who switch differ with respect to
opportunity structure _(capacity and diversity)_ in all fields except language and cultural studies. Among graduates from all fields, more switchers had no university
in close proximity.


**4** **Results**


First, we ran the empty models for the two independent variables and found
intraclass correlation coefficients (ICC) between 0.208 and 0.370 for the different fields of studies, which means that between a fifth and more than a third of the
field-specific variance in transition rates lies between individual institutions. The
only exception is the transition to another university for graduates of language
and cultural studies, where the ICC of 0.071 is markedly lower. Thus, the data
structure warrants and requires the multilevel approach. Fig. 2 shows the AMEs
of the logistic multilevel analysis of the explanatory variables on transition to
master (model 1a-d; for complete results see Table A1 in the appendix). Among
the individual-level variables, we find that _men_ are less likely to continue with
a master’s degree in all fields of study. Higher _age,_ non-academic _educational_
_background_ and previous _educational pathways_ other than _Abitur_ reduce the likelihood to take up a master’s degree, while _school performance_ exerts an influence
only among graduates in language and cultural studies. The indicators of _local_
_academic integration_ (jobs, internships) have no effect (with the exception of a
small influence of internships in engineering). Better _bachelor grades_ and _for-_
_eign experience_ increase the likelihood of a master’s degree across all fields. A
more negative (higher) rating of study quality reduces the master probability in
all fields, .... all fields, while a good _tutoring evaluation_ has a small effect only
in languages and cultural studies.

**Fig.2** (continued)


70 S. Falk et al.


Among the university-level variables, _university type_ is very strongly
associated with the transition to a master’s programme, as has been expected
(except in language and cultural studies, where universities of applied sciences
play only a minor role). In institutions with stronger _research orientation,_ measured by percent of third-party funded staff, individuals are more likely to take up
a master’s degree in language and cultural studies, in law, economics and social
sciences, and maths and natural sciences, while there is no such connection in
engineering. The _diversity_ of master’s programmes only has an effect in law, economics and social sciences, where more varied offers are associated with a higher
transition probability compared to the same number of master and bachelor studies. The _capacity,_ in contrast, has a small effect only in language and cultural
studies and a somewhat larger one in maths and natural sciences – here, however,
a larger capacity is associated with a lower transition probability.
Next, we ran similar multilevel models with the same set of explanatory factors
on the likelihood of pursuing a master’s degree at some other higher education
institution than the home university (models 2a-d; complete results in Table A2
in the appendix). As can be seen in Fig. 3, _men_ are less likely to change to
another university, while _age_ does not make a difference. An academic _parental_
_background_ and the _Abitur_ increase the likelihood, whereas _school performance_
does so only among graduates in language and cultural studies. A better _bachelor_
_grade_ is associated with a higher likelihood to stay at the same university in
two fields of study (loosely in law, economics and social sciences; strongly in
engineering), while measures of local integration show inconsistent effects: _Study-_
_related jobs_ are associated with staying at the home university only for language
and cultural studies, while _internships_ and _foreign study_ experience make a switch
more likely in all fields of study. The evaluation of _quality of study_ does not make
a difference (except for a small effect in engineering), but having experienced
_good tutoring_ increases the likelihood to stay across all fields of study. The role of
_university type_ is not straightforward: While university graduates in language and
cultural studies are less likely to switch, university graduates in engineering are
more likely to do so, even if the effect is only small. Also, the research strength
indicated by proportion of _third-party funded staff_ exerts significant influence
only in language and cultural studies, with higher research strength reducing the
likelihood of switching. A more diverse local opportunity structure, manifest in
the _diversity_ dimension, increases the likelihood to stay in two fields of study
(language and cultural studies; law, economics and social sciences), while the

_capacity_ dimension and _distance to the next university_ have an effect only in

**5** **Discussion and conclusion**


In this paper, we examined the factors contributing to graduates’ decisions of
whether and where to continue their university education. Our focus was on
the university characteristics that may induce a graduate to continue for a master’s degree and to stay on at the bachelor university for doing so. We find
that a substantial part of the field-specific variation in both decisions resides
between individual universities. In the multilevel model we demonstrated that, in
line with our hypotheses, individual-level academic performance, parental background, field of study and previous educational pathways have an impact on
these decisions. Again in accord with our assumptions, we observed an additional contribution of perceptions of study quality and tutoring support on both
decisions.
Some individual- and institutional-level factors exert the same influence in
all fields of study, while others show effects only in one or more fields. This
documents the importance of analysing contexts with respect to the interplays
between various dimensions of differentiation, such as university type, field of
study and individual institution’s characteristics.
Among institutional characteristics, the distinction between research universities and universities of applied sciences turns out to be the main factor for all
fields of study: graduates from research universities take up a master’s programme
and also change to another institution more often than graduates from universities
of applied sciences. These effects are strong and pervasive even when controlling
for relevant individual and university characteristics that vary between university
types. But the effects of university type on changing to another university also
depend on field of study, as especially graduates of engineering from universities
of applied sciences are more likely to switch.
A stronger research orientation of the home university makes graduates more
likely to take up a master’s programme in all fields of study, whereas the likelihood of changing to another university is affected only in languages and cultural


11 In light of the strong influence exerted by university type, it would be interesting to calculate separate models for research universities and universities of applied sciences as a robustness check and to uncover patterns only present within the two types. The data structure,
however, does not permit running separate multilevel models. Results of ordinary logistic
regression models on the other hand cannot be compared directly due to the method change.


74 S. Falk et al.


studies. Effects of the local structure of master opportunities yield a complex
pattern. The number of available study places seems to play a role mainly in language and cultural studies; the range and variety of available offers is influential
in language and cultural studies as well as law and economics.
Contrary to our initial assumptions and to previous findings, a shorter distance
to other universities is not generally associated with a higher probability of
switching universities; it plays a role only for graduates of languages and cultural studies. This suggests that our initial assumption – that the need to relocate
is a bigger obstacle than the costs associated with changing an institution per
se – might have been erroneous, and that the hurdle lies largely in the change of
institution itself. Scheller et al. (2013) found that taking up a master’s programme
at another university entails markedly longer delays and frequently requires meeting additional admission criteria or a documentation of skills. This points to a
pronounced friction between procedures and/or requirements of different universities. Reducing these frictions, therefore, might help to facilitate mobility between
universities, an explicit goal of the Bologna Reform, even within Germany. [12]

A number of potentially relevant variables could not be included in our analyses, because they were not part of the graduate survey. One of them is the question
whether graduates had been mobile before taking up their bachelor’s programme,
since earlier mobility enhances the likelihood of later mobility (Falk & Kratz,
2009). Other variables concern characteristics of the university that graduates
change to for their master studies (Ohlendorf et al., 2022 in this volume). While
we deliberately focused on the context of higher education experience at a given
institution and not on the regional context, research integrating both perspectives within a rational choice framework could provide further insight into how
various contextual layers interact with individual factors in bringing about mobility decisions at educational crossroads. Additionally, the measures constructed at
institutional level need to be discussed and refined in empirical trials. For example, a more elaborate operationalization of the distance measure could take into
account the profiles of universities in the vicinity or the fact that thresholds may
vary substantially from student to student, depending on their access to transportation (Frenette, 2006), or may vary between regions with different transportation
infrastructure.
Another methodological question is how the second-level units are conceptualized. While universities as a whole certainly play a major role in attracting and


12 Of the transitions to other universities, 44.0 percent occurred within Bavaria, 39.3 percent
were transitions to universities in other parts of Germany and 16.8 to institutions in another
country (most often Austria).


Going on for the master’s degree. Does the university make a … 75


selecting students, as well as in shaping individual study experience, they are also
highly diversified internally. The diverse internal structures in many ways differ
between different institutions. Organisational theories have characterized universities as “loosely coupled systems” (Hüther & Krücken, 2018), which suggests
that other or additional levels of analyses, such as faculties, departments or even
individual chairs, must be considered as carriers of relevant characteristics. Also,
higher-level units such as fields of study and professional identities or organisations, should be considered. By conducting our analyses separately for fields of
study, we managed to reveal some disciplinary idiosyncrasies. The development
of a more differentiated and theory-based approach to the construction of relevant levels of analysis could enhance this understanding considerably. A general
challenge for the research into inter-university differences, however, lies in the
high demands on the data and data structure. Data sets with the properties required for multilevel analyses are not abundant in Germany, and universities (or
other higher-level units of analysis such as federal states) are often reluctant to
let researchers examine or expose differences. Improvements in data collection
and availability are therefore essential for a better understanding of institutional
differences and their causes at several levels of analysis.
At present a central challenge is the lack of objective, reliable, valid and
available indicators that would allow a grouping of universities (or other units
of analysis) according to potentially relevant common attributes that may either
attract certain students or exert different socializing influences, beyond the binary
distinction of university types. This lack can be traced back to a long-standing
assumption of equality: Apart from distinguishing research universities and
universities of applied sciences, there was little acknowledgement or active development of differences in the quality of research and teaching or competition for
students (Hüther & Krücken, 2018). More evidence is available from countries
where personal investments in education are high and universities compete for
paying students (Agasisti & Johnes, 2010; English & Umbach, 2016; HemsleyBrown, 2015) and where, consequently, highly transparent outcome measures and
university profiles are available for analysis. However, the situation in Germany
has begun to change in the course of the Bologna Process. While initiatives to
develop profile and reputation on the basis of teaching performance are slow in
gaining ground, more profiling activities occur with respect to research profile and
research excellence, and there is already some evidence that individual enrolment
decisions can be influenced by these aspects (Weiss & Schindler, 2017). Due
to demographic changes, at least some lower-profile institutions in less urban or
prestigious locations will have to invest more in order to attract enough students
to maintain their size and weight (The Expert Council’s Research Unit, 2019). It
is therefore possible that the picture will have changed in a couple of years.
