Institute for Employment Research and Christian-Albrechts-Universitty Kiel, Kiel,
Germany
[E-Mail: Annekatrin.Niebuhr@iab.de](mailto:Annekatrin.Niebuhr@iab.de)


A. Otto · A. Rossen
Institute for Employment Research, Nuremberg, Germany
[E-Mail: Anne.Otto@iab.de](mailto:Anne.Otto@iab.de)


A. Rossen
[E-Mail: Anja.Rossen4@iab.de](mailto:Anja.Rossen4@iab.de)


C. Teichert
Institute for Employment Research and Leibniz University Hanover, Hanover, Germany
[E-Mail: Christian.Teichert@iab.de](mailto:Christian.Teichert@iab.de)



© Der/die Autor(en), exklusiv lizenziert durch Springer Fachmedien
Wiesbaden GmbH, ein Teil von Springer Nature 2022
M. Jungbauer-Gans und A. Gottburgsen (Hrsg.), _Regionale Mobilität und_
_Hochschulbildung,_ Higher Education Research und Science Studies,
[https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-658-36156-3_5](https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-658-36156-3_5)



115


116 A. Niebuhr et al.


for these factors, the likelihood of staying in the university region does not
significantly differ between German and international graduates. In contrast,
foreign graduates who attended school in Germany show a higher propensity
to leave the university region in order to enter the labour market than the other
graduate groups, conditional on covariates.


~~**Keywords**~~


Migration [•] Mobility [•] German graduates [•] Foreign graduates [•] International
graduates [•] Work experience [•] Duration analysis


**1** **Introduction**


Human capital is a key determinant of the economic development of a society
as well as of individual labour market outcomes. An extensive literature investigates the importance of human capital, with university graduates receiving special
attention in this field of research. The findings of various studies indicate that
high-skilled workers contribute to regional economic development by increasing
the local knowledge base and fostering innovation (e.g., Faggian & McCann,
2008; Gennaioli et al., 2013; Winters, 2013).
Higher education institutions (HEI) can play a crucial role for the regional
accumulation of human capital and hence regional growth. However, universities
and other HEI will make a significant contribution to local human capital only
if a substantial percentage of their graduates remains in the university region
(Haapanen & Tervo, 2012; Harrison & Turok, 2017). In other words, the region
which is financing higher education may not always benefit from HEI, and this
may cause underinvestment in a country such as Germany, where a significant
percentage of funding comes from the subnational level (Haussen & Uebelmesser,
2018a). For regional policy makers it is therefore of interest to obtain information on the proportion of “stayers” and on the question whether the probability
of staying in the region differs significantly across graduate groups. Moreover,
measures aiming to promote graduates’ employment in the local labour market
call for detailed knowledge on the factors that influence migration decisions of
university graduates.
The extensive empirical literature on the mobility of university graduates
often focuses on interregional migration patterns of graduates in certain countries and on mobility that is linked to labour market entry (e.g. Dotti et al.,
2013; Haapanen & Tervo, 2012; Krabel & Flöther, 2014). This literature provides comprehensive evidence that individual, study-related and regional factors


Post-study migration behaviour: Differences between native … 117


impact migration decisions after graduation (see the recent overviews in Buenstorf et al., 2016; Corcoran & Faggian, 2017). But so far, little is known about
differences in migration behaviour across different groups of graduates and the
factors that give rise to such variation.
Differences in migration behaviour can emerge between graduate groups if
their compositions vary systematically with respect to important determinants of
mobility. This should be the case for domestic and foreign graduates as well as
different ethnic groups because of their different migration experiences and different connections with the local labour market. Several studies show that these two
factors significantly influence the migration behaviour of university graduates.
Greenwood (1972) as well as Faggian et al. (2007) examine disparities across
ethnic groups and provide evidence for graduates in the US and the UK. Comprehensive findings on graduate groups who completed their studies in Germany,
however, are lacking. This applies in particular to differences between foreign and
domestic graduates and is in sharp contrast to the increasing importance of the
former group in Germany. [1] Besides, knowledge on international graduates’ propensity to take up employment in the local labour market could enable regional
policy makers to better address this graduate group in order to reduce the shortage
of skilled labour in Germany (Glorius, 2016; Griesbeck & Heß, 2016).
The present study aims to contribute to the understanding of differences in
migration behaviour across different groups of university graduates. Based on
the social network approach (Granovetter, 1974), we hypothesize that foreign
graduates show a comparatively high propensity to leave the university region
after graduation because, due to their relatively short stay, they might be less
integrated in the region. Pre-study mobility may thus be an important predictor of
post-graduation mobility. [2] In addition, we assume that job-relevant networks and
work experience partly explain differences in migration behaviour because they
influence the region where graduates look for a job and consequently migration
decisions (Krabel & Flöther, 2014).
In the empirical analysis, we investigate whether the probability to leave the
university region after graduation differs significantly between German and foreign graduates. More precisely, we distinguish three groups of graduates, based
on their citizenship and on where they obtained their university entry certificate:


1 In 2019, more than 10 percent of graduates in Germany had foreign citizenship. This
proportion has doubled over the last two decades (Statistisches Bundesamt, 2020b).
2 We use the terms _mobility_ and _migration_ interchangeably. In the empirical analysis, we
examine out-migration from the university region.


118 A. Niebuhr et al.


(1) German graduates, who finished secondary school in Germany and have German citizenship, (2) foreign graduates, who finished school in Germany and have
foreign citizenship, and (3) international graduates, who obtained their university
entry certificate abroad and are foreign nationals. We focus on the interregional
migration of graduates within Germany because we have no information on
graduates who leave the country after their final exams.
To analyse the post-graduation migration propensity of different graduate
groups we employ event history methods. We use individual-level data that links
information from student records of six German universities with administrative
data compiled in social security records to investigate the propensity to migrate
when taking up the first full-time position. The sample comprises about 35,000
students who completed higher education in the period from 1996 to 2012. The
advantage of this data is that it provides information on both the nationality
and the country/region where the higher education entrance qualification was
obtained. This allows us to distinguish German, foreign and international graduates and to determine pre-study mobility. As the data comprises graduates’
employment histories, information on job characteristics (e.g., part-/full-time,
occupation) and places of work as well as the region of residence, we can identify
the length, location and type of work experience before and during studies.
The paper is organised as follows: Section 2 briefly outlines theoretical considerations and existing empirical evidence on graduate migration, with a focus
on differences in migration behaviour between foreign and domestic graduates.
Sect. 3 describes our methodology and the data set. Results of the empirical
duration analysis as well as some descriptive results are discussed in Sect. 4, and
Sect. 5 is the conclusion.


**2** **Theoretical considerations and state of research**


**2.1** **Theoretical considerations**


The literature on graduate migration mainly resorts to human capital theory
(Becker, 1964) and signalling theory (Spence, 1973) to explain the relationship between individual and study-related factors and migration decisions of
highly educated young people. Regional science, on the other hand, highlights
the importance of the regional environment for the migration behaviour of young
workers (e.g. Buenstorf et al., 2016). However, differences in migration behaviour
between ethnic groups or between domestic and foreign graduates are rarely considered in these strands of literature. Damelang (2007) examines the mobility of


Post-study migration behaviour: Differences between native … 119


foreign and domestic workers. He argues, on the one hand, that differences in the
migration behaviour of different ethnic groups may result from the composition
of these groups with respect to factors that influence migration behaviour. On
the other hand, the author presumes that foreign workers also pursue different
strategies of job search and exhibit a lower migration propensity than domestic
workers because they have fewer job-relevant contacts and hence less information
on regional labour markets.
In his seminal work, Granovetter (1974) indeed emphasizes the importance
of social contacts for finding employment. Such contacts enable job seekers to
collect more information on the availability of jobs as well as job characteristics
and this informational advantage should allow them to find better employment. In
a similar vein, Kramarz und Nordström Skans (2014) stress the relevance of networks for the labour market entry of young workers. A considerable proportion
of graduates in Germany has already acquired work experience before and/or
while attending university (see Teichert et al., 2020). Contacts with colleagues
and employers at these workplaces appear to be a good source of relevant information for later employment. According to DaVanzo (1983) such work-related
social capital is highly location-specific, and location-specific work experience
may affect the migration decisions of graduates when looking for a job after
finishing university (Teichert et al., 2020).
The propensity to migrate after graduation may be different for foreign and
domestic graduates because they are integrated in job-relevant networks to different degrees, as proposed by Damelang (2007). As international graduates have
left their country of origin to enter university, they can only have made contacts
relevant for finding jobs while they were studying. In contrast, graduates who
obtained their university entrance qualification in the country of their university had the chance to establish work-related networks through jobs held before
starting university (e.g., apprenticeship training) and later on during higher education. [3] Graduates who attend university in their home region should be particularly
well-integrated and less inclined to take up employment elsewhere as they have
already accumulated location-specific knowledge via contacts with family members, friends etc. and through previous schooling. Since international graduates
cannot benefit from similarly dense local networks in the university region they
may be more inclined to enter the labour market elsewhere.
Finally, pre-study mobility should be an important factor explaining differences in the post-graduation migration behaviour of foreign and domestic


3 Scheller et al. (2013) note that more than 20 percent of all first-year students at German
universities have completed an apprenticeship when enrolling for higher education.


120 A. Niebuhr et al.


graduates, because highly educated young people may benefit from previous
migration experience when searching a job after studies. This applies particularly to international graduates, who have left their country of origin for studying
abroad (Faggian et al., 2006). In contrast, a considerable percentage of domestic
graduates originates from the hinterland of the local university (especially in Germany) and lacks mobility experience before studies (Krabel & Flöther, 2014). [4]

There is robust evidence in the literature that earlier migration experience strongly
predicts the probability of migrating at a later point (see e.g. Faggian et al., 2007;
Krabel & Flöther, 2014 for evidence pertaining to graduate migration).


**2.2** **Empirical evidence**


The extensive empirical literature on student and graduate migration mainly
investigates factors that impact the migration decision. [5] One group of analyses focuses on regional factors and uses information on the aggregate level to
explain interregional migration flows. For example, Faggian und McCann (2008)
investigate the interdependence of graduate migration and the innovation performance of regions using a simultaneous equation model. Other studies employ
gravity models to investigate the determinants of migration flows of students and
graduates (e.g. Delisle & Sheamur, 2010; Dotti et al., 2013; Faggian & Franklin,
2014).
A second group of studies uses individual-level information and focuses on
the probability of migrating after graduation. While some analyses examine
graduates’ choice of destination based on multinomial logit models (e.g. Gottlieb & Joseph, 2006; Haussen & Uebelmesser, 2018a), others apply probit and
logit models to identify individual, study-related and contextual factors that influence the decision to leave the university region and determine different types of
migration behaviour (e.g. Faggian et al., 2006, 2007; Krabel & Flöther, 2014). As
our analysis is related to the latter group, we will focus on these studies below.
Within this strand of research there are studies that investigate differences
in the post-graduation migration behaviour of different groups of young workers. Detang-Dessandre und Molho (2000) or Faggian et al. (2007), for instance,


4 In our data, 44 percent of the graduates obtained their higher education entrance certificate
in the university region (see Sect. 3). Stöver und Sibbertsen (2018) show that in seven out
of sixteen federal states more than 50 percent of the high school graduates moved to another
state to start university.
5 Recent publications by Corcoran und Faggian (2017) and Buenstorf et al. (2016) provide
excellent overviews.


Post-study migration behaviour: Differences between native … 121


examine gender differences. Few authors, however, compare different ethnic
groups or foreign and domestic graduates. [6] An early study by Greenwood (1972)
for the US suggests that White graduates are more mobile than non-White. Faggian et al. (2007) investigate the role of ethnicity in the migration behaviour of
university graduates in the UK. Their results confirm the evidence provided by
Greenwood (1972), as they find that non-White ethnicities show a relatively low
propensity to migrate in the early stages of their career.
Several studies examine the interregional migration of university graduates
in Germany. However, none of these analyses focuses on differences between
domestic and foreign graduates, although some authors include citizenship as
a control variable in their regression models. Busch und Weigert (2010) argue
that a higher migration propensity among foreign graduates might result from
an inclination to return to the country of origin after graduation. In their investigation, though, they cannot differentiate between first- and second-generation
immigrants or between students who finished secondary school in Germany or
abroad. Their regression results suggest that domestic and foreign graduates do
not differ significantly in the probability to leave the university region. This is in
line with recent evidence provided by Teichert et al. (2020).
Analyses on graduate migration in Germany demonstrate the significance of
factors such as social networks and work experience, which could be particularly relevant for explaining differences between foreign and domestic graduates.
For instance, Krabel und Flöther (2014) as well as Haussen and Uebelmesser
(2018a) show that migration decisions of graduates in Germany are impacted by
contacts with local employers and work experience. This is confirmed by findings
reported in Teichert et al. (2020), which indicate that work experience gained in
the university region before and during higher education reduces the likelihood
of outmigrating after graduation, while extra-regional work activities increase the
probability of a labour market entry outside the region of study. In a similar vein,
Haussen and Uebelmesser (2018b) note that the propensity of outward migration
is significantly lower when graduates have been employed in the school or university region. Finally, the findings by Busch und Weigert (2010) indicate that
graduates are also more likely to stay in the university region if their family ties
are strong. [7]


6 We refrain from giving a detailed review of the literature on the international mobility
of students and graduates because our analysis focuses on interregional mobility within
Germany.
7 Furthermore, studies on international migration show that the probability of return migration decreases when migrants have resided in the host country for a longer period of time
(e.g., van den Berg & Weynandt, 2013).


122 A. Niebuhr et al.


To summarize, previous studies on graduate migration in Germany do not
analyse differences between German and foreign graduates in detail as they only
tend to control for citizenship. The present study combines citizenship with information on the region where the university entrance qualification was obtained.
This enables us to consider three different groups and to investigate the role of
pre-study mobility in more detail. Moreover, previous analyses provide no evidence on the factors that underlie differences in the mobility behaviour of the
above-mentioned graduate groups.


**3** **Data and empirical strategy**


**3.1** **Data**


For our analysis, we use a comprehensive data set that combines information from
the student records of six medium-sized German universities and universities of
applied science with data from the Integrated Employment Biographies (IEB)
of the Institute for Employment Research (IAB). Both data sets were merged
through a record linkage procedure which uses a set of individual identifiers
available in each data base. Teichert et al. (2020) provide a detailed description
of this combined data set focusing on graduate migration. Several other studies
also employ this data to investigate graduates’ employment biographies before,
during and after studies (e.g., Agethen, 2019; Neu, 2016).
The student records include information on individual characteristics such as
gender, nationality, date of birth, mobility before enrolling as well as study-related
characteristics such as duration of study, date of completion, final grade, type of
degree and subject. To determine pre-study mobility, we can only use information
on the region/country where the graduates obtained their university entry certificate. There is no information on earlier migration events in our data set and, in
particular, no information on the country of birth. However, it is important to note
that in our analysis migration to the university region may involve international
as well as interregional migration.
The IEB contains daily information on unemployment, benefit receipt and
employment for all workers covered by the social security system. Job characteristics (type of employment, occupation, industry) and the region of workplace are
available as well. The combined data set allows us to identify migration events
after graduation and to accurately measure work experience before and during
studies.


Post-study migration behaviour: Differences between native … 123


To define different groups of young workers we combine two characteristics
of graduates. Based on the information on citizenship and the place where they
obtained their university entry certificate we differentiate: (1) German graduates,
who finished secondary school in Germany and have German citizenship, (2) foreign students, who finished school in Germany and have foreign citizenship and,
finally, (3) international graduates, who obtained their university entry certificate
abroad and are foreign citizens. [8]

We focus on mobility after the last degree earned by each graduate. As the
information provided in the IEB exclusively refers to employment in Germany,
we analyse graduates’ interregional migration within Germany only. The data
contains no information on migration to another country after graduation. Hence
we may underestimate the post-graduation mobility of international graduates as
we cannot observe return migration to their countries of origin or labour market
entry in a third country.
We exclude graduates who are subject to mobility restrictions in Germany.
Due to specific regulations, it is difficult for teachers, for example, to work in
a publicly maintained school in another federal state than the one where they
graduated. Graduates older than 35 at the time of graduation are also excluded. [9]

The final sample consists of 34,717 graduates who completed higher education
between 1996 and 2012. We can trace their further careers in the IEB until 2016.
Thus, we have an observation period of at least four years after graduation and a
maximum of 20 years for the analysis of mobility after graduation.
We use information on the daily workplace available in the IEB to define
a move. To determine a migration event, we use periods of employment of at
least one year outside the university region. Thus, we do not consider short-term
absences in our analysis that are due, for example, to an internship. Furthermore,
we use functional labour market areas to demarcate the university region. Migration is thus defined as relocation across the borders of these functional regions,
which consist of several counties (NUTS 3 level). As commuting takes place
mainly within functional regions, we reduce the problem of graduates taking
up employment outside a narrowly defined university region without actually
relocating. Within our framework this is not identified as a migration event.


8 We cannot identify persons with dual nationality in our data. Furthermore, a potential fourth
group of graduates, persons with German citizenship who obtained their university entry
certificate abroad, is not represented in the data.
9 We assume that the mobility behaviour of this group differs significantly from that of
the majority of graduates. These older graduates are likely to have entered the labour market already before starting university. Moreover, we assume that there are important differences with respect to family status, a characteristic we do not record.


124 A. Niebuhr et al.


The focus of our analysis is on pre-study mobility, job-relevant networks and
work experience as predictors of post-study migration and as factors that may
cause significant differences in the migration behaviour of the three groups considered here. As migration experience, we consider migration to the university
region only, because the data set lacks information on earlier migration events.
To measure the work experience of each graduate, we cumulate all their employment spells before and during studies and distinguish different types of work
experience. First, regular employment (RE), which is subject to social security
contributions, is distinguished from marginal employment (ME), i.e. temporary
and occasional part-time jobs with a limited number of working hours. Second,
we differentiate according to the place where work experience was obtained:
within or outside the university region. Finally, we identify graduates who
completed a vocational training before starting university.


**3.2** **Empirical strategy**


As the data structure is probably right-censored, we use survival analysis to investigate differences in the migration behaviour of German, foreign and international
graduates. This method also enables us to control for duration dependence and
the potential correlation between graduate characteristics and the length of a spell
of residence in the university region. Our estimates of the likelihood to migrate
could be biased if we ignored an important duration dependence and its correlation with individual and/or study-related characteristics (see Andrews et al., 2011
and Cleves et al., 2010 for a more detailed discussion).
To model the mobility behaviour of the three different graduate groups, we
examine the hazard rate of migration (see e.g. Teichert et al., 2020). Formally,
the hazard rate _hi_ _(t)_ is the probability of migration given that the graduate _i_ has
not moved by the time _t_ after graduation:


_hi_ _(t)_ = Pr _(Ti < t_ + 1| _Ti_ ≥ _t)_ (1)


where _Ti_ is the length of a residence spell in the university region. We estimate
a proportional hazard model in order to identify important determinants of the
mobility decision:


_hi_ _(t_ | _xi_ _)_ = _h_ 0 _(_ t _)_ exp _(xi_ β _)_ (2)


Post-study migration behaviour: Differences between native … 125


where _h_ 0 _(t)_ is the baseline hazard and _xi_ is a vector of individual, studyrelated, employment and regional characteristics. In particular, we include dummy
variables for the different graduate groups introduced in Sect. 3.1 to determine
corresponding differences in migration behaviour. For a binary variable _xi_ the
effect of a change from 0 to 1 is given by [exp _(_ β _)_ - 1] and measures the impact
relative to the baseline hazard _h_ 0 _(t)_ (see Cameron & Trivedi, 2005). A positive
coefficient β points to a positive effect on the hazard rate of migration, i.e. the
stay in the university region terminates faster, ceteris paribus.
Individual, study-related and biographical variables are constant over time, but
characteristics of the university region are time-varying control variables. The
more comprehensive models also include time effects and fixed effects for the
university, field of study and type of degree. For a detailed list and description of
all explanatory variables, see Tab. 3.
A semi-parametric Cox model is often applied in event history analyses.
However, a disadvantage of this model in the present setting is that the baseline hazard is not parametrised and not estimated, i.e. the model does not
provide explicit information on duration dependence. Therefore, following Teichert et al. (2020) and Andrews et al. (2011), we estimate a parametric model
and assume that the baseline hazard can be described by a Weibull distribution
( _h_ 0 _(t)_ = _pt_ _[p]_ [−][1] ) [10] :


_hi_ _(t_ | _xi_ _)_ = _pt_ _[p]_ [−][1] exp _(xi_ β _)_ (3)


The shape parameter _p_ indicates whether the hazard rate increases or decreases
exponentially with time. Since the estimates might be affected by unobserved
influential factors at the individual level, we include a frailty term _ui_ that captures
unobserved heterogeneity:


_hi_ _(t_ | _xi_ _, ui_ _)_ = _pt_ _[p]_ [−][1] _ui_ exp _(xi_ β _)_ (4)


Applying this model, we assume that graduates differ randomly with respect to
migration behaviour and that _ui_ is independent of the observed characteristics _xi_
(Cameron & Trivedi, 2005). With this frailty model, we consider that observations
for a given graduate are correlated. When _ui >_ 1 _(ui <_ 1 _)_, the individual risk of

outward migration of graduate _i_ is larger (smaller) than for the average graduate.
The frailties are unobservable positive quantities and are assumed to have a mean
of 1 and the variance _θ_ . The estimate of _θ_ is used to measure the degree of
within-person correlation and is displayed in the regression tables (see Sect. 4.2).
It is important to control for unobserved heterogeneity in this analysis because
our data does not contain information on further factors that may affect postgraduation migration behaviour, such as parental educational background and
graduates’ family status. These factors may also correlate with different explanatory variables. Moreover, the frailty term enables us to account for unobserved
heterogeneity within the large group of German graduates.


Post-study migration behaviour: Differences between native … 127


**4** **Empirical analysis**


**4.1** **Descriptive analysis**


Figure 1 shows survival functions, which are based on non-parametric KaplanMeyer estimates, for the three different groups of graduates. The survival rate
specifies the probability of staying in the university region until time _t_ after the
beginning of the respective residence spell. We observe the majority of migration
events within a period of six to seven years after graduation. However, between
50 and 60 percent of the graduates leave the university region within the first
two years after their final exams (see also Buenstorf et al., 2016; Teichert et al.,
2020). Moreover, the long-term proportions of “stayers” are significantly different
among international graduates and those who finished secondary school in Germany. 17 years after graduation the difference between international graduates
and the two groups who finished school in Germany amounts to almost seven
percentage points; less than 13 percent of the international graduates still work
in the university region and have never left to take up a full-time job. In contrast,
we observe no significant differences between German and foreign graduates who
obtained their university entry certificate in Germany.
In the following, we provide descriptive evidence on factors that may influence the propensity to migrate after graduation. If the composition of the graduate
groups differs significantly with respect to important determinants of migration
behaviour, these composition effects might explain the differences in the proportion of stayers shown in Figure 1. Teichert et al. (2020) as well as Krabel and
Flöther (2014) show that the propensity to stay in the university region varies
significantly with fields of study. Tab. 1 displays the distribution of graduates over
fields of study and reveals that there is some variation between the three groups. [11]

Interestingly, international students seem to choose certain disciplines more frequently than the other groups, disciplines which were found to be associated with
above-average post-graduation mobility, such as Business Administration & Economics as well as Mathematics & Computer Science (see Krabel & Flöther, 2014;
Teichert et al., 2020). However, the pattern is not clear-cut, as we also observe
an above-average proportion of Humanities, for which migration probabilities are
lower compared to the above-mentioned fields. In all, it seems unlikely that the
differences in proportions of stayers are caused solely by composition effects
with respect to the fields of study.


11 These results closely resemble the distribution across fields of study in official administrative university data, see Statistisches Bundesamt (2020a).


128 A. Niebuhr et al.

|---|---|---|---|

|Humanities|14.0|14.5|14.5|
|Geography &<br>Meteorology|3.5|3.0|3.0|
|Mathematics &<br>Computer Science|6.8|11.1|11.1|
|Pharmacy|2.2|1.9|1.9|
|Engineering|13.1|9.2|9.2|
|Natural Sciences|10.1|18.6|18.6|
|Psychology|4.7|2.3|2.3|
|Law|4.7|3.0|3.0|

|Others|1.2|0.7|0.7|


Source: University panel linked to the Integrated Employment Biographies (IEB) of the
Institute for Employment Research (IAB); authors’ own calculations


A second potentially important composition effect could result from the work
experience accumulated before and during studies in Germany, because work
experience is likely associated with social networks established at the workplace
and with the accumulation of information that might be helpful for job search
after graduation. Depending on where work experience was acquired, inside or
outside the university region, it may either reduce or increase the probability of
leaving the university region after graduation (see Teichert et al., 2020). We distinguish between three types of work experience (vocational training, marginal
and regular employment), consider the location of the workplace (inside or outside the university region) and whether the experience was gained before or
during studies.
The results in Table 2 indicate that the three groups of graduates differ with
respect to the work experience accumulated before and during higher education
in Germany. For instance, more than 20 percent of the German graduates in our


Post-study migration behaviour: Differences between native … 129


sample completed vocational training before enrolling at the university, whereas
the percentage is much lower among foreign and, in particular, international graduates. We observe a similar pattern for other types of work experience (regular
and marginal employment, measured in days) before studies, although differences between German and foreign graduates tend to be less clear-cut. These
findings change if we consider work experience that was acquired during studies:
As expected, the number of working days declines and marginal employment
inside the university region prevails. There is a slight indication that the university region might be more important for international students as a location where
work experience is accumulated.
Another factor that could cause important composition effects is pre-study
mobility. There is ample evidence in the literature on graduate migration that
prior migration experience is a strong predictor of post-study mobility (see for
example Faggian et al., 2007; Krabel & Flöther, 2014). Information on the region
where the students obtained their university entry certificate allows us to identify
the occurrence of migration before studies (see Sect. 3). It is evident that all
international students possess migration experience. 53 percent of the German
students in our data set moved to the university region after finishing school.
Foreign graduates are less mobile, with only 35 percent changing their residence
to start university.
To summarize, the probability to leave the region of study after graduation is
likely to vary across the three groups as their composition differs with respect to
factors that should affect the propensity to migrate.


**4.2** **Regression results**


In this section, we present the regression results for different continuous-time
duration models. Our dependent variable refers to the probability to leave the
region of study and is defined as taking up a full-time job outside the (functional)
university region. The explanatory variables are described in Tab. 3. In order to
get some information on the potential factors underlying differences in mobility
behaviour identified in Sect. 4.1, we start with a simple regression model that
only includes the dummy variables for two groups of young workers, the foreign
and the international graduates. We then successively add groups of explanatory
variables and examine how these changes in the specification affect the estimates
of the two dummy variables.
The regression results in Table 4 refer to two models that include individuallevel factors only. Model 1 shows the raw differences in the hazard rate of

[(exp(0.200) - 1) × 100] compared to German graduates, our reference group,
while there are no significant differences between German and foreign graduates.
According to model 2, female graduates tend to enter the labour market with
a full-time job in the university region more often than males. However, this
effect is not robust if we add study-related factors and regional characteristics
(see Tab. 5). The estimates for age and age squared indicate that taking up the
first post-graduation job outside the university region becomes more likely as the
age of the graduates increases, but the size of the positive effect decreases. [12]

Mobility before starting university turns out to be the most important control for the differences between graduate groups. Faggian et al. (2007) argue that
previous migration events are likely to indicate individuals for whom mobility
imposes fairly low psychological and other costs. We identify a strong migrationenhancing impact of pre-study mobility that is well-documented in the existing
literature. Haussen and Uebelmesser (2018b) and Krabel und Flöther (2014)
provide corresponding evidence for Germany. [13]

More importantly, the estimates of the group dummies change significantly
when the information on previous migration is included in the regression
model. Both effects change the sign while the difference between German and
international graduates remains significant. If we only add gender and age in the
regression, the signs of the group dummies do not change. Pre-study mobility is
thus the decisive factor. While the descriptive analysis points to an above average mobility of international graduates, model 2 suggests that their probability to
leave the university region is relatively low (hazard −23 percent) once we control
for previous migration experience. In contrast, foreign students seem to be more
mobile in the extended model, but the effect is not precisely estimated and is
statistically significant at the 10 percent level only.
In Table 5, we successively add further groups of explanatory variables, differentiating between study-related factors (model 1), work experience
(model 2) and regional controls (model 3). The inclusion of study-related factors causes minor changes in the differences between German, foreign and
international graduates, although the estimates indicate that exam grade, study
duration, type of degree as well as field of study significantly correlate with the

likelihood to leave the university region. However, we refrain from a detailed

14 As a robustness check, we also included interaction terms between exam grades and fields
of study in the three models. The findings on differences between the graduate groups are
not affected by this extension of the models.


136 A. Niebuhr et al.


Instead we focus on the effects of work experience and how its inclusion
impacts differences between the graduate groups. The employment biography and
particularly work experience gathered prior to and during studies turn out to be
important factors that affect post-graduation mobility. [15] The descriptive analysis
in Sect. 4.1 has shown that a significant proportion of the young German and
foreign workers finished a vocational training before starting university and that
these two groups as well as international graduates experienced significant periods
of marginal or regular employment. Depending on where the work experience
was gained, these periods of employment can reduce or increase the probability
of outward migration.
Integration of work experience in the regression models affects particularly the
difference between German and international graduates. It seems that the lower
probability of outmigration that still obtains in model 1 goes back to differences
in work experience. This implies that, in relative terms, international graduates
tend to accumulate more work experience in the university region than German
students. Given that work experience results in social networks and knowledge
about labour markets, the spatial scope of these two factors seems to be more
limited to the university region for international students compared to the other
groups. Moreover, it is noteworthy that foreign graduates show a higher probability to leave the university region after final exams than the other groups,
conditional on a broad range of migration determinants (see model 3).
The regression results indicate that the different types of work experience
reduce or increase the probability of outward migration, depending on where it
was gained. Completing vocational training in the university region before enrolment significantly increases the probability to take up the first job in this local
labour market. Applying the results of model 3, a local apprenticeship decreases the hazard by 24 percent ([1 – exp(0.278)] × 100), while an extra-regional
apprenticeship increases the propensity to leave the region of study by 30 percent.
Apart from the effects of marginal employment in the university region before
studies, all estimates are in line with this pattern. The effect of marginal employment does not correspond to the theoretical arguments outlined in Sect. 2.1, as we
would expect a dampening effect on the probability to leave the university region
after graduation. However, the effect is moderate: extending marginal employment before studies by 100 days increases the hazard of migration by merely
2 percent. We interpret this result as indicating that there are graduates with a
general, above-average propensity to move. We detect the strongest effects for


15 We refer to a more detailed discussion of the corresponding effects in Teichert et al. (2020).


Post-study migration behaviour: Differences between native … 137


regular employment during studies, with effects ranging between 38 and 62 percent. Finally, taking up a job with a previous employer after graduation frequently
implies that labour market entry takes place inside the university region. Thus,
previous employers are often companies located in that region.
We refrain from a detailed discussion of duration dependence as indicated by
the corresponding parameter of the Weibull distribution (implied _p_ ). The estimates point to a positive duration dependence as they are significantly larger
than 1 in all models. This indicates that the probability of taking up a full-time
job outside the university region increases, the longer the individual stays in that
region. Teichert et al. (2020) argue that this may be due to young workers who
initially search for a job in the region of study, but extend their area of job search
if they fail to find (adequate) employment in the local labour market.


**5** **Conclusions**


A large body of empirical literature provides evidence on the importance of highskilled workers for regional economic development (e.g., Faggian & McCann,
2008; Gennaioli et al., 2013). University graduates and their migration decisions
receive special attention within this field of research. But while many studies
investigate the factors that influence the migration decision of graduates, little is
known about differences between different groups of graduates in Germany and
about differences between domestic and foreign/international graduates in particular. This is surprising as the size of the latter group(s), and hence their potential
importance for regional economic development, has significantly increased over
the last decade (Glorius, 2016; Griesbeck & Heß, 2016).
Our findings indicate that international graduates staying in Germany are more
inclined to leave the university region for labour market entry than young highskilled workers who finished secondary school in Germany. These differences
are associated with migration experience. When pre-study mobility is controlled
for, students from abroad more often tend to stay in the university region after
graduating. Our regression results suggest that this is due, at least partly, to the
accumulation of work experience. These findings contradict the hypothesis that
social networks are generally less available to international students. Other composition effects seem to play a lesser role for the differences in post-graduation
mobility between German, foreign and international graduates.
Theoretical arguments by Granovetter (1974) and DaVanzo (1983) suggest that
the impact of work experience reflects the significance of social networks and


138 A. Niebuhr et al.


location-specific knowledge for job search and migration behaviour after graduation. Our findings indicate that the university region as the location of a workplace
before and during studies seems to be more important for international students
than for the other groups. This implies that the international students who stay
in Germany have gained comparatively more local contacts and more information on the local labour market. This in turn increases their probability of taking
up their first job after graduation in the university region, ceteris paribus. When
we control for work experience before or during studies we find no significant
differences between international and German graduates. However, it is important to keep in mind that we tend to underestimate the post-graduation mobility
of international graduates, as we have no information on those young workers in
our data set who return to their home country or enter the labour market in a
third country.
Interestingly, there is some indication that foreign graduates more likely leave
the university region after final exams conditional on important migration determinants. This is somehow in contrast to evidence provided by Faggian et al.
(2007) and Greenwood (1972) who show that non-White graduates tend to be
less mobile than White graduates in their early career in the US and the UK.
However, the comparability of these findings is limited because the set-up of
the studies differs and institutional differences between countries may affect the
results.
From a regional policy perspective, international graduates may have received
too little attention, although their number has increased significantly in Germany. Given the high percentage of international graduates leaving the region to
enter the labour market, local policy makers probably regard chances of keeping these young highly educated persons in the region as low. Our findings
suggest, however, that providing opportunities to gain work experience in the
local labour market might be a promising strategy to increase the proportion
of stayers among international graduates. This recommendation also applies to
other graduate groups, of course, because local work experience tends to reduce
the likelihood of outmigration irrespective of citizenship and previous migration
experience. However, as international graduates may face more obstacles when
contacts to employers in Germany are concerned they may profit particularly from
policy measures that aim at promoting contacts with local firms and offering work
experience in the local labour market.
