##### **International students in higher** **education: Intentions to leave the host** **country after graduation**

Theresa Thies


~~**Abstract**~~


In the light of an increasing need for skilled labor in Germany, this
paper examines whether international students with third-country citizenship
(non-EU/EFTA) differ from students with EU/EFTA citizenship in their intentions to leave Germany after graduation. Such differences are expected, as
international students from third countries often originate from regions with
lower economic development as well as lower political and societal security.
Further, individual financial or security considerations could explain potential
differences in their intentions to leave. The question is answered using data
from a Germany-wide survey of international bachelor’s and master’s students.
The study shows that students with third-country citizenship do not differ
from students with EU/EFTA citizenship in their intentions to leave. However,
third-country nationals indicate less often that they are uncertain about leaving Germany. Although characteristics of the home countries mostly do not
influence return migration intentions, students who came to Germany in order
to live in safety within a peaceful environment report lower intentions to leave
Germany after graduation.


~~**Keywords**~~


International students [•] Return migration [•] Spatial mobility [•] Higher
education


T. Thies (B)
Bavarian State Institute for Higher Education Research and Planning (IHF), Munich,
Germany
[E-Mail: Thies@ihf.bayern.de](mailto:Thies@ihf.bayern.de)



© Der/die Autor(en), exklusiv lizenziert durch Springer Fachmedien
Wiesbaden GmbH, ein Teil von Springer Nature 2022
M. Jungbauer-Gans und A. Gottburgsen (Hrsg.), _Regionale Mobilität und_
_Hochschulbildung,_ Higher Education Research und Science Studies,
[https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-658-36156-3_6](https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-658-36156-3_6)



143


144 T. Thies


**1** **Introduction**


In the face of aging populations, immigration is essential for most European
countries to keep up functioning labor markets, health care, and social security
systems. Due to similar demographic challenges and increases in life quality in
most European countries, Fuchs et al. (2019) predict a decline in migration within
Europe within the next 15 years. Consequently, immigration from third countries [1]

will become more important for European countries in the future (Fuchs et al.,
2019). In Germany, the number of skilled labor migrants from non-EU countries has already been rising steadily for years (BAMF, 2020b). How to recruit
highly qualified migrants is a much-debated policy issue (Diehl & Preisendörfer,
2007; Seibert & Wapler, 2020). Germany has continuously been trying to facilitate immigration and labor market access of highly qualified migrants, e.g., by
passing the Immigration Act in 2005 or the Skilled Immigration Act in 2020.
International graduates from German higher education institutions are a relevant and valuable group for the German labor market. First, they are essential for
covering the demand for skilled labor. Many international students study subjects
whose occupational fields are affected by the shortage of skilled labor. In 2019,
50.4% of the international students studied science, engineering, or mathematics,
and 5.2% human medicine or health sciences (DAAD & DZHW, 2020). Second,
compared to other migrant groups, it is easier to integrate these graduates into
the German labor market, as they have already gained social and cultural capital,
such as intercultural skills, social contacts, language skills, and country-specific
knowledge (Diehl & Preisendörfer, 2007). Some students also possess experience
on the German labor market due to student employment and internships. Further,
they do not need to get their higher education qualification recognized because
they possess a degree from a German educational institution. Third, the number of
international students has continuously increased over the last 10 years to 276,122
in 2019 (DAAD & DZHW, 2020). Most international students (70%) plan to stay
after graduation, while only 30% intend to leave Germany (Lokhande, 2017).
However, results of a survey of new immigrants in Germany show that compared to other immigrant groups, international students have a lower intention to
stay in Germany (Diehl & Preisendörfer, 2007). A reason for this may be that
students have gained individual resources facilitating international mobility, such
as language skills, a generalizable human capital, and international regulatory


1 Individuals are defined as third-country nationals if they are not citizens of an EU-28 or
an EFTA country (Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, and Switzerland). This study focuses on
EU-28 countries (including the United Kingdom, as the data were collected in 2017 and 2018
when the United Kingdom was still a member of the EU).


International students in higher education … 145


competence (Diehl & Preisendörfer, 2007). From a political and economic perspective, it is important to make Germany attractive to international students as a
permanent place of residence. Hence, it is necessary to understand under which
conditions international students turn from temporary migrants into permanent
residents after graduating.
Although some previous German and international studies focused on
international students’ intentions to stay in their host countries (e.g., Bijwaard &
Wang, 2016; Bratsberg, 1995; Koenings et al., 2020; Soon, 2010), few of them
have paid attention to the influence of the immigration policy on intentions to
stay (e.g., Bijwaard & Wang, 2016). As regulations regarding residence rights
after graduation are country-specific, international findings are difficult to transfer to the context of other countries. Residence and work permits are often linked
to nationality: In Germany, graduates with EU or EFTA (Iceland, Liechtenstein,
Norway, and Switzerland) nationality enjoy freedom of movement and have the
same access to the labor market as German students, whereas graduates from third
countries face restrictions regarding residence permits and labor market access
(see Sec. 3). At the same time, international students from third countries often
originate from economically less developed or politically and societally insecure
countries. Previous studies have concentrated on economic considerations accompanying the decision to stay (e.g., Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Koenings et al., 2020;
Soon, 2010) rather than on considerations regarding the home country’s political
and societal security. [2]

This paper will examine whether students from third countries differ in their
intentions to stay from students from EU/EFTA countries at the beginning of their
studies. If there is a difference, it will be explored whether the association remains
significant even after controlling for the home countries’ economic development
(gross domestic product (GDP) per capita (p.c.) and GDP p.c. growth) and political and societal security (Global Peace Index). Moreover, it will be examined
whether individual financial or security considerations when coming to Germany
explain the effect. Similar to previous studies (Koenings et al., 2020; Soon, 2010),
this paper will also take into account that at the beginning of their studies some
students are uncertain whether to stay or return. It is important to study this group
of students in more detail because suitable policy measures could persuade them
to stay or go, depending on what is intended by the respective host countries’
policymakers.
This article is structured as follows: Based on the human capital model
of migration, Sect. 2 provides a theoretical overview of international students’


2 An exception is Bratsberg (1995), who includes the civil rights index in his estimation.


146 T. Thies


decision-making concerning the question of staying in or leaving the host country. Sect. 3 explains residence rights after graduation in Germany, which form
the framework for residence-related considerations. Sect. 4 summarizes the state
of research on international students’ intentions to return to their country of origin. In the empirical part of the study, the research question will be answered
by applying a multilevel model to the first two waves of the International Student Survey (Falk et al., 2021). The survey includes full-time degree-seeking
international bachelor’s and master’s students from over 100 higher education
institutions in Germany. Finally, the limitations of the study will be outlined, and
the results will be discussed.


**2** **Theoretical considerations**


Previous studies emphasize that international students make two decisions: first,
the decision to migrate to a host country, and second, the decision to stay in or
leave the host country (Dustmann & Glitz, 2011). Consequently, the first migration decision is temporary (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Lu et al., 2009) and can,
under specific conditions, turn into a permanent migration decision when students
decide to remain in the host country. To stay in the host country after gaining
an education and labor market experience is likely for migrants, as skills and
education earned in one country may be difficult to transfer to other countries
(Constant & Massey, 2002).
Earlier research on international students has focused primarily on the human
capital model of migration as an explanatory theory for both migration decisions
(Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Koenings et al., 2020; Soon, 2010). According to Sjaastad (1962), individuals migrate because they aim to invest in the productivity of
their own human capital. After an individual monetary and non-monetary cost–
benefit analysis, individuals will move if the expected benefits exceed the costs.
While the monetary costs are direct costs of the relocation, the non-monetary
costs include opportunity costs (e.g., the time needed to find and train for a new
job) and psychic costs (e.g., leaving family and friends). The costs of return
can also be monetary (e.g., higher earnings) and non-monetary (e.g., locational
preference for a place of residence). Sjaastad (1962) notes that barriers to the
free movement of labor restrict the optimal allocation of human resources. The
model explains selective migration processes, as revenues in the places of origin and destination can vary with individual characteristics (e.g., occupational
group, education, or age) (Kalter, 1997). According to the theory, the labor market returns in the home or host country to skills acquired in the host country


International students in higher education … 147


will affect international students’ return migration decisions. For example, the
human capital model suggests that younger students are inclined to return to
their home country because they may profit from a potentially higher income
in the home country for a longer period (Kalter, 1997; Kley, 2009). On the
other hand, previous monetary and non-monetary investments (e.g., in cultural
capital or social integration), which should increase with time spent in the host
country, could lower the expected lifetime skill returns in the home country. Furthermore, graduates from fields with high employment rates and income in the
host country should tend to stay, especially if employment rates and incomes are
lower in the home country. The human capital model of migration is in line with
macro-economic theories, which predict that individuals will move to economically better-performing regions because they expect higher skill returns (Kley,
2009).
Previous studies have discussed two main motives underlying the first migration decision of international students that may also impact the decision on return
migration: First, students may migrate because of less favorable educational
opportunities in their home countries (e.g., number of educational institutions,
costs, or quality of education). They seek an excellent education abroad to invest
in their human capital and to acquire skills more effectively, with the ultimate goal
of leaving the host country to maximize returns to their skills in the home country (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Dustmann & Glitz, 2011; Rosenzweig, 2006). It is
conceivable, for example, that students from developed countries (e.g., EU countries) study abroad to improve their career opportunities in their home countries
and return home after graduating (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016). Second, students
may migrate because they anticipate low labor market returns to their education in the home country. Completing an education in the host country is seen
as a possibility to integrate and stay permanently in a country with high skill
returns (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Rosenzweig, 2006). For example, labor market
integration is easier for citizens from third countries when they have a German
university degree.
The human capital model of migration has been criticized because it treats
differences in income as the primary determinant of migration (Kalter, 1997).
In the case of international students, a highly skilled migrant group, the wish to
invest in their human capital and to increase skill returns are plausible motives for
migration. However, there are other possible initial motives: Catastrophic changes
in the environment (e.g., war) may also directly affect decisions on migration and
return migration (Sly, 1972). The security of the home and host country needs to
be considered when explaining migration behavior. Wolpert (1965) adds a socialpsychological perspective to the model, stating that individual perceptions of the


148 T. Thies


places of origin and destination are important for the decision to move. Moreover,
he notes that every individual assigns his or her place of origin a “place utility”. If
the place utility decreases, the individual will not necessarily move but can adapt
to the changed environment instead. Drawing on Wolpert’s (1965) ideas, Kalter
(1997) sees migration as a three-staged process: Based on an initial situation, an
individual may develop thoughts and intentions of moving, which may lead to an
actual migration decision. Kalter (1997) notes that individuals usually see their
place of origin as the only option for action in the initial situation. Developing
thoughts of moving means that individuals perceive migration as part of a set of
alternative actions.
However, migration intentions may be accompanied by uncertainty. Uncertainty in migration decisions can occur when individuals do not have full
information about the advantages or disadvantages of moving (Saarela & Rooth,
2012). At the beginning of their studies, international students may lack information about current and future possibilities of integration into the labor market
in their home and host countries. During their studies, however, initially uncertain students can collect information that may help them to decide on a possible
return migration.
To conclude, economic and security differences between the places of origin and destination drive migration behavior. However, individual characteristics
(e.g., occupational group, age) and individuals’ perceptions of the places of origin
and destination also affect intentions to move. A country’s integration and immigration policy set limits to personal wishes, considerations, and decision-making
processes, regulating conditions under which graduates can stay in the host country and are granted residence and work permits. As immigration and integration
policies depend on the host country and affect the decision on return migration, the following section will describe the residence regulations for international
graduates in Germany.


**3** **Residence rights in Germany after graduation**


After graduation, international students in Germany typically continue their studies in a master’s or Ph.D. program or transit to the labor market. Students from
EU and EFTA countries possess similar rights as German students for extending
their studies in Germany and for access to the German labor market. They do not
need a residence or work permit.
Graduates from third countries who wish to start a master’s or Ph.D. program
need to extend their residence permit for educational purposes (Residence Act,


International students in higher education … 149


2020, s. 16b). When individuals with a student residence permit wish to enter
the labor market, they can extend their residence permit for the purpose of jobseeking for 18 months following the date of graduation (Residence Act, 2020,
s. 20). While looking for a job, which needs to be related to their studies, graduates are allowed to work in order to support themselves. After finding a job
that secures their livelihood, graduates must apply for a residence permit for the
purpose of qualified employment (Residence Act, 2020, s. 18b). Having held this
residence permit for two years to work in a job commensurate with their qualification, they can, under certain conditions, obtain a permanent residence permit
(Residence Act, 2020, s. 18c). If graduates with third-country citizenship go back
to their home country first and come back to Germany for work later, they can
apply for a jobseeker’s residence permit, which is limited to six months. Holders
of such a permit are not allowed to work (exception: max. 10 h per week probationary employment as part of the application procedure) and must secure their
subsistence (Residence Act, 2020, s. 20).
Based on these residence regulations, it seems advisable for students from
third countries to stay directly after graduation if they plan to migrate to Germany
permanently. Due to the limited time for job-seeking, they should start applying
for study-related jobs early on. In the following section, the pertinent literature
will be reviewed to examine the individual characteristics and considerations
affecting international students’ return intentions.


**4** **Previous research**


Few international (Baruch et al., 2007; Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Bratsberg, 1995;
Huang, 1988; Kruanak & Ruangkanjanases, 2014; Lu et al., 2009; Soon, 2010)
and German (Koenings et al., 2020; Lokhande, 2017) studies have focused on
return intentions of international students. They find that the intentions to stay or
leave a country form through an interplay of social aspects, gained cultural capital, and socio-demographic aspects with political and economic considerations
and conditions.


**4.1** **Empirical findings on intentions to stay or to leave**
**the host country**


Not much is known about the association between a _country’s immigration and_
_integration policy_ and international students’ intentions to stay. In Germany,


150 T. Thies


residence rights differ for individuals with an EU/EFTA and a third-country
nationality, as described in section 3. Diehl and Preisendörfer (2007) show that
immigrants from new EU countries, other European countries, and the world have
higher intentions to stay in Germany than immigrants from EU-15 countries, even
after controlling for employment and political/societal reasons to move. This may
be because EU citizens can commute to Germany at any time, while immigrants
from third countries need a residence permit to be allowed to work in Germany
(Diehl & Preisendörfer, 2007). Koenings et al. (2020) note that running separate
regressions for EU and non-EU countries on international students’ intentions to
stay yielded quantitatively similar results.
More is known about the _economic, political, and societal security aspects_ that
accompany decisions to move. Results for Germany show that a GDP p.c. growth
in the home country decreases the intention to stay in Germany (Koenings et al.,
2020). These findings are in line with two studies from the United States that
rely on administrative data (Bratsberg, 1995; Rosenzweig, 2006). A higher GDP
p.c. of the home country (Bratsberg, 1995) or higher monetary rewards to an
individual’s skills in the home country (Rosenzweig, 2006) are associated with a
lower probability that a student will become a permanent resident of the United
States. Moreover, few students return to home countries with a low civil rights
index (Bratsberg, 1995). Besides the home and host countries’ economic and
political development, the individual perception and assessment of these developments are essential. If students have a positive perception of the host country’s
quality of life, career and labor market possibilities, intentions to stay there are
higher (Baruch et al., 2007; Lokhande, 2017). If the work environment, skill-use
opportunities, and the labor market situation in the home country are rated positively, on the other hand, intentions to stay in the host country decline (Baruch
et al., 2007; Soon, 2010). If the home country’s lifestyle is viewed positively,
intentions to return increase (Soon, 2010).
When it comes to _social aspects,_ partnership, family, and friends are important
for students’ decisions to stay in the host country. Having a partner in the host
country or a partner with the host country’s nationality increases intentions to
stay (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Koenings et al., 2020; Lokhande, 2017). Also,
familial support for the stay abroad and the feeling of having social support from
students and teachers increase intentions to stay in the host country (Kruanak &
Ruangkanjanases, 2014; Lokhande, 2017; Soon, 2010).
Concerning gained _cultural capital,_ it was found that with more time spent
in the host country and longer study duration, students have higher intentions to
remain in the host country (Koenings et al., 2020; Lokhande, 2017; Soon, 2010).
The use of the German language and the feeling of being welcome in Germany


International students in higher education … 151


were not associated with intentions to stay in previous studies (Koenings et al.,
2020; Lokhande, 2017). However, a successful adjustment process to living and
studying in the host country positively affects intentions to stay (Baruch et al.,
2007; Kruanak & Ruangkanjanases, 2014). Furthermore, having acquired practical work experiences related to one’s studies on the labor market in Germany
increases the probability of staying (Lokhande, 2017).
_Socio-demographic aspects_ as well influence intentions to stay: With increasing age, students are more likely to remain in the host country (Koenings et al.,
2020; Lu et al., 2009). Younger age groups have a higher probability of returning to their home country (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016). Males and females do not
differ in their intentions to stay when controlling for other confounding variables
(Koenings et al., 2020; Lokhande, 2017; Soon, 2010).
Regarding _study-related aspects,_ Koenings et al. (2020) find that master’s students have lower intentions to stay compared to bachelor’s students. Further,
graduates with an average graduation grade are more likely to stay than graduates with an above-average grade (Lokhande, 2017). Concerning the area of
studies, it has been found that students from the fields of economics, business,
law, natural sciences, and engineering have higher intentions to stay in Germany
than students of the social sciences (Koenings et al., 2020).


**4.2** **From intentions to behavior**


Most of the existing studies have concentrated on evaluating determinants of
intentions to leave (Baruch et al., 2007; Koenings et al., 2020; Kruanak &
Ruangkanjanases, 2014; Lu et al., 2009; Soon, 2010) rather than predictors of
actually leaving (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Bratsberg, 1995; Rosenzweig, 2006).
As international students are a highly mobile migration group, it is difficult to
obtain valid information on their residence after graduation based on survey data
(Koenings et al., 2020).
Rosenzweig (2006), Bratsberg (1995) and Bijwaard and Wang (2016) have
evaluated the migration behavior of international students using administrative
data, modeling the change from a temporary student visa to a permanent visa.
Administrative data are not affected by attrition or selectivity and provide large
samples. However, they lack information on study-related and individual characteristics. Additionally, limiting the analyses to international students with a
student visa or a student residence permit neglects the fact that international students in Germany may have other visa or residence statuses or may not need a
visa or a residence permit (e.g., EU/EFTA students).


152 T. Thies


Studies relying on survey data have shown that initial return intentions at
the beginning of a student’s degree program are associated with a higher probability of return intentions during studies (Soon, 2010). The intention to stay is
related to a six times higher probability of staying in the host country (Lokhande, 2017). Previous researchers also take into account that the intentions to
stay are uncertain at the beginning of one’s studies. Thus, Soon (2010) analyzes
the four response options “Definitely not return”, “Probably not return”, “Probably return”, and “Definitely return”. In contrast, Koenings et al. (2020) calculate
uncertainty models based on the indicated certainty about intentions to stay. Furthermore, Koenings et al. (2020) discuss when to measure students’ intentions
during their studies. They point out that measuring the intention to stay at the
beginning of the studies has methodological benefits: At that point, the estimation
is not influenced by students dropping out of university, which leaves a specific
sample after a couple of semesters. Second, knowing why students would want
to leave the host country at the beginning of their studies can help to develop and
implement measures to influence their later decisions.


**5** **Hypotheses**


It is expected that students from third countries are less likely to have intentions
to leave Germany or to be uncertain about leaving after graduation compared to
students from EU/EFTA countries. Third-country citizens are expected to be less
likely to leave due to residence-related, economic, and security reasons.
With respect to residence-related reasons, the theoretical model of Sjaastad
(1962) suggests that barriers to the free movement of labor may affect migration processes. Residence regulations provide a strong incentive for international
students from third countries to stay in Germany immediately after graduation:
At that point, they can easily extend their residence for up to 18 months for the
purpose of job-seeking. If they leave Germany and come back later, on the other
hand, they have only six months for job search and are not allowed to work. As
they have 18 months after graduation to find a job in Germany which is related
to their studies, students from third countries usually need to start looking and
applying for a job already during their bachelor’s or master’s studies. Hence, they
need to decide earlier about return migration and consequently should be more
certain about their plans even at the beginning of their studies. In contrast, students from EU/EFTA countries can easily decide to leave or return to Germany
at any time (Schittenhelm & Schäfer, 2019).


International students in higher education … 153


Regarding economic and security reasons, macro-economic theories suggest
that individuals are drawn to economically better-performing regions. Based on
the human capital model of migration (Sjaastad, 1962), returning to the home
country is beneficial if the individual’s lifetime earnings in the home country
exceed lifetime earnings in the host country. As Germany is a high-income country, returning to the home country may not be beneficial for students from less
developed countries. Furthermore, the wish to integrate into the German labor
market may have been the reason for coming to Germany in the first place
(Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Rosenzweig, 2006). Entering the German labor market is often easier for third-country migrants when they possess a degree from
a German institution. Existing literature has critically discussed the phenomenon of “brain drain” (Baruch et al., 2007; Kruanak & Ruangkanjanases, 2014;
Lokhande, 2017): The international competition for skilled labor draws highly
educated individuals or individuals with high educational aspirations from underdeveloped countries to already economically strong countries. Many international
students from third countries originate from regions with lower economic development and lower political and societal security. Most international students in
Germany in the year 2018 come from China, India, Austria, Russia, Syria, and
Iran (DAAD & DZHW, 2019). China, India, Russia, Syria, and Iran are not classified as high-income countries in 2017 based on the World Bank categorization
of gross national income per capita, in contrast to Austria and many other European countries (World Bank, 2020c). Syria and Iran are two countries marked by
political conflicts, as the high numbers of accepted asylum applications in Germany in 2017 show (BAMF, 2020a). Furthermore, China, India, Russia, Syria,
and Iran can be considered less peaceful states, based on the Global Peace Index
(IEP, 2017). Consequently, it is expected that indicators of economic development
and security of the home country explain a potentially negative association between coming from a third country and wanting to leave Germany. Finally, the
individuals’ perceptions of the places of origin and destination are crucial for
the decision to move (Wolpert, 1965). Individual reasons for coming to Germany, such as hoping to improve financial or security-related living conditions,
should decrease the intentions to leave Germany. They should also explain the
association between third-country citizenship and return migration intentions.


154 T. Thies


**6** **Method**


**6.1** **Data and sample**


The analysis uses data from the International Student Survey _._ These data
were collected in the framework of the project [3] “Success and withdrawal of
international students in Germany” (for a methods report, see Falk et al., 2021).
International students are bachelor’s and master’s students who gained their university entrance qualification abroad and enrolled as full-time degree-seeking
students at a German university or university of applied sciences in the winter semester 2017/2018. 125 cooperating universities distributed the link for the
registration among these students. About 14% of all international students at the
125 universities registered for the study (N = 4,751). Towards the end of the
first and second semester, the registered students received an e-mail asking them
to answer an online questionnaire, either in German or English. 3,828 questionnaires were completed in the first wave, 3,270 questionnaires in the second wave.
As the same students are invited every semester, the data structure is longitudinal. The survey does not include questions concerning the purpose of residence.
Consequently, the dataset may include international students possessing a residence permit for the purpose of study, for family, humanitarian, legal, or political
reasons, or students with other types of residence purposes. Among the latter are
students who had already been living in Germany. They may have decided to
start university because they see a German higher education degree as an opportunity to integrate into the German labor market and to settle in a secure and
economically well-developing country.
The sample consists of students who completed the first wave of the survey
and answered the question on return intentions in the second wave. Individuals
with missing values on the relevant variables were excluded from the analysis.
Furthermore, individuals with a second nationality or with a diverse gender identity were excluded, which left a final sample of N = 2,955 students. Students
with a second nationality were excluded because the home country’s higherlevel indicators were to be matched to the individual data. In these cases, it
was unclear which country was perceived as the home country. Also, there are
not enough students with a diverse gender identity to construct a meaningful
reference category.


3 The project, supported by the Federal Ministry of Education and Research, has the funding
code 01PX16016B. Responsibility for the contents of this publication rests with the author.


International students in higher education … 155


**6.2** **Operationalization**


The two dependent variables are based on a question that was asked in the
second wave: “At the beginning of the winter term 2017/18, did you intend to
stay in Germany after graduation?” with the answer options “Yes”, “No” and
“I don’t know”. For the first dependent variable, the answer option “Yes” (N
= 1,673) is used as a baseline and the intention of leaving Germany (“No”)
(N = 403) is estimated. In total, 2,076 students are included in the estimation
predicting intentions to leave Germany (see Table 1). For the second dependent
variable, again, the answer option “Yes” (N = 1,673) serves as a baseline, and
the probability of being uncertain (“I don’t know”) (N = 879) is estimated. 2,552
students are included in the regressions predicting uncertainty (see Table 2). Students who intended to stay in Germany are chosen as a reference category, as, in
light of the shortage of skilled labor in Germany, it is crucial to explore which
skilled migrants residing in Germany plan to leave the country or are unsure
about leaving.
Regarding _residence-related, economic, and security aspects,_ a variable is constructed that indicates whether a student is a national of an EU/EFTA country
or of a third country. [4] Further, three macro-level variables from external data
sources are inserted into the regression. The GDP p.c. (current US$) for 2017
and the GDP p.c. growth for 2017 (annual percent) of the country of a student’s
nationality are added to the model from World Bank (2020a, b) as indicators of
economic development. As an indicator of security in the home country in 2017,
the Global Peace Index is included in the model (IEP, 2017). The index is recoded
in such a way that higher values indicate higher security. The index is a suitable
measure because it captures three domains of security in the home country: (1)
ongoing domestic and international conflicts (e.g., ongoing civil and international
wars), (2) societal safety and security (e.g., criminality and political instability),
and (3) militarization (e.g., economic resources allocated to the military, military
operations). Two individual-level items indicating the wish to improve financial
or security-related living conditions as a reason for migrating to Germany are
included in the model. It was asked how important certain aspects were for the
decision to study abroad. The variable _studying, living affordable_ is based on the
item “… because studying and living in Germany is more affordable than in my
home country”. The variable _living in safety_ is based on the item “… to live in
safety within a peaceful environment”. The lower two categories (not important


4 As the data are clustered by country of first nationality, the variable third country is a higherlevel unit characteristic.


156 T. Thies


at all, rather unimportant) are compared to the upper categories (neither important
nor unimportant vs. rather important, very important).
To control for gained _cultural capital,_ self-rated proficiency in the German
language is inserted in the regression as most jobs on the German labor market require sufficient mastery of the language. The variable was measured on a
scale from one (beginner) to seven (native competence). Second, a variable is
included in the model which indicates whether the student has lived in Germany
before or not (“Before your current stay in Germany, have you ever lived in
Germany for a period of at least one month?”). To capture the _social aspects,_
frequency of contact with German students was assessed (never, several times
a month; several times a week; once or several times a day). Parents’ approval
of the students’ stay abroad was measured with the item “It is very important
to my parents that I study in Germany” (rather applies, fully applies; neutral;
does rather not apply, does not apply at all). Furthermore, a variable is added
to the estimation that indicates whether the student has a partner in Germany,
abroad, or no partner. Concerning _study-related aspects,_ the final high school
grade is included as an indicator of ability. The variable is adapted to the German grading system. [5] Furthermore, it is z-standardized based on the distribution
of grades in German Studienkollegs [6] or in the home countries. Due to many
missing values for this variable (see Table A1), students are categorized into
upper-, middle-, and lower-performing thirds and students with missing information. A variable is generated that indicates whether students study for a master’s
degree (vs. bachelor’s degree). A variable capturing field of study is added to
the model. It measures whether a student studies mathematics, natural science, or
engineering versus other non-STEM fields (humanities; sports; legal, economic
and social sciences; human medicine; agricultural, forestry, and food sciences;
veterinary medicine; art; other). Regarding _socio-demographic aspects,_ gender
(female vs. male) and age are added to the estimation.


5 _N_ = 1 + 3* _PmaxPmax_ ~~−~~ - _PminP_
N = Grade point average in the German system; P = Grade point average awarded in
home country/Studienkolleg; Pmax = Upper benchmark home country/Studienkolleg; Pmin
= Lower benchmark home country/Studienkolleg.
6 Prospective international students whose university entrance qualification is not recognized
in Germany must attend a German Studienkolleg for two semesters. At the Studienkolleg,
they receive a German university entrance qualification if they pass an assessment test.

**6.3** **Analytical procedure**


The research question will be analyzed using a multilevel model, as the structure
of the data is hierarchical: i = 1,…, nj students are nested in j = 1,…, M home
countries. Students from the same country may share similarities and thus are not
independent of each other, so that ignoring the clustering of the data would lead
to underestimated standard errors (Snijders & Bosker, 2012). A two-level logistic
random intercept model is fitted (Rabe-Hesketh & Skrondal, 2012; Snijders &
Bosker, 2012):

_h_ =1 _[β][h]_ _[x][hi j]_ [ +] _h_



�12

(1)
_h_ =13 _[β][h]_ _[z][hj]_ [ +] _[ U]_ [0] _[ j]_



yij is the binary dependent variable of the return intentions, which takes on
the value 1 if a student intends to leave Germany or is uncertain about leaving.
xhij are the individual-level characteristics, and zhj display the country-level characteristics which are included in the model. _U_ 0 _j_ is the random group-dependent
intercept (Rabe-Hesketh & Skrondal, 2012; Snijders & Bosker, 2012). The interclass correlation for the logistic random intercept model is described by the
formula (Snijders & Bosker, 2012):


ICC = var _(U_ 0 _j_ _)_ (2)

**7** **Results**


**7.1** **Descriptive statistics**


The N = 2,955 international students in the sample are, on average, 25 years old
(see Table A1). 44% are female, and 62% are studying in a master’s degree program. 43% of the students are enrolled in a non-STEM subject (e.g., humanities,
sports, legal, economic and social sciences, art, human medicine, agricultural or
food sciences). 85% originate from a third country.
ThemajorityofstudentsexpressawillingnesstostayinGermanyaftergraduation
(57%) (see Table A1). About 30% do not know yet, and 14% plan to leave. Students
intending to leave have less often lived in Germany before starting a study program.
26% of the respondents planning to leave state that they have a partner abroad, this

is true for only 13% of those who plan to stay. Students intending to leave are, on

Third-country nationals plan to stay in Germany more often: Of the students
who intend to stay, 88% come from a third country. Of those intending to leave,
77% have a third-country nationality. Respondents in the sample who intend to
leave Germany more often come from countries with a higher GDP p.c., GDP p.c.
growth and peace index compared to persons who intend to stay. They less often
indicate that they migrated to Germany for financial or safety reasons than students who intend to stay (see Table A1). At the same time, international students
from third countries more often originate from countries with a lower GDP p.c.
and a lower peace index (see Table A2). Correspondingly, respondents from third
countries more often state that they came to Germany for financial or security
reasons than students from EU/EFTA countries.

test shows that the multilevel logistic regression is significantly different from an
ordinary logistic regression (p < 0.001). Consequently, it is the preferred model.
Model 1 indicates that students from third countries do not differ significantly
from EU/EFTA students in their intention to leave Germany (p > 0.05). However, the direction of the coefficient signals that students from third countries
have lower intentions to leave (p < 0.1). Model 2 includes the three macro-level
variables of the home country’s economic and security situation. None of them
is significant (p > 0.05). The direction of the GDP p.c. coefficient indicates that
originating from a country with a high GDP p.c. is associated with a higher intention to leave while controlling for the other confounding variables (p < 0.1). [7]

Model 3 includes the individuals’ economic and security considerations when
migrating to Germany. Having immigrated in order to live an affordable life is
not associated with intentions to leave Germany. However, students who express
that they migrated to Germany because they wanted to live in safety (rather agree
or strongly agree) have lower intentions to leave (10 percentage points) than students for whom living in safety was less important. The association remains
significant even after controlling for cultural capital and social, study-related, and


7 In the following, confounding variables are always controlled for, even if not expressly
stated in each interpretation.


162 T. Thies


socio-demographic aspects in model 4. The individual-level effects included in
model 4 further show that with a one-unit increase in German language skills,
the intention to leave decreases by 2 percentage points on average. Students who
lived in Germany for at least one month before their current stay indicate a lower
intention (6 percentage points) to leave than students with no previous residence
experience. Moreover, the probability of leaving is 7 percentage points lower for
students with a partner in Germany than for single students. For students with a
partner abroad, the probability of leaving is 11 percentage points higher. Being
an engineering student is associated with a 6 percentage points lower intention
to leave than being a non-STEM student. A one-unit increase in age is, on average, associated with a lower probability of leaving (1 percentage point). Further,
female students show a higher probability of leaving Germany.
Second, an empty (variance component) model is fitted, predicting who is
still uncertain about return migration compared to students who want to stay in
Germany. The ICC value shows that 6 % of the unexplained variance in being
uncertain is located at the country level. The likelihood-ratio test indicates that
the hypothesis of the variance of the random intercept being zero can still be
rejected (p < 0.001). According to model 1, students from third countries show
a lower probability of being uncertain about leaving (11 percentage points) than
students from EU/EFTA countries.
In model 2, the country-level characteristics are included, but they cannot
explain the effect of third-country nationality. Coming from a country with a high
GDP p.c. and a country with a high peace index does not affect the probability
of being uncertain about leaving or staying while controlling for the other confounding variables. Being a national of a country with a high GDP p.c. growth is
associated with a 2 percentage points lower probability of not knowing whether to
stay or leave. Model 3 includes the individuals’ economic and security considerations when migrating to Germany, which again cannot explain the negative effect
of originating from a third country. The wish to improve one’s financial living
conditions by moving to Germany is not associated with uncertainty about leaving or staying. Students who indicate that living in safety was a rather or very
important reason for migrating to Germany have a lower probability of being
uncertain about leaving (7 percentage points) than students for whom living in
safety was less important. Even after including indicators of cultural capital accumulation and social, study-related, and socio-demographic aspects, the negative
effect of third-country citizenship stays significant.
The student-level coefficients and significances in model 4 are comparable to
the predictors of intentions to leave: A one-unit increase in German proficiency


International students in higher education … 163


is associated with a lower uncertainty about return intentions on average (2 percentage points). Further, students who lived in Germany for at least one month
before their current stay and students with a partner in Germany have a lower
probability of being uncertain about leaving than students with no previous residence experience and single students. A one-year increase in age is, on average,
associated with a 1 percentage point lower probability of being uncertain.


**7.3** **Limitations and robustness check**


The first limitation of this study is that individuals with double nationality were
excluded from the analysis in order to match the macro-level indicators to students’ home countries and to be able to cluster respondents according to countries
of origin. This is unfortunate, as this procedure excludes an especially transnational migrant group, i.e., individuals who, due to their dual citizenship, can stay
in different countries temporarily or permanently. However, descriptive statistics
show that there are only small differences in intentions to return between students with one and two nationalities. Of the 127 students with double nationality,
54% state that they want to stay and 12% that they want to leave Germany after
graduation, 35% do not know yet. Of the students with single nationality, 57%
intend to stay, and 14% want to leave Germany (see Table A1).
As a robustness check, the regressions shown in Tables 1 and 2 were estimated
including both students with single and double nationality. The results did not
change substantially. [8]

Second, an alternative to the mixed logistic model could be a multinomial
logistic regression with clustered standard errors, which was estimated as a
robustness test. It takes into account that the total probability of choosing all
outcome categories may not be larger than one (Kühnel & Krebs, 2010). Similar to the presented results, third-country nationals were found not to differ in
their intentions to leave Germany from students with EU/EFTA citizenship, but
to be less often uncertain at the beginning of their studies. The latter effect is not
explained by country-level or individual-level characteristics. Only small changes
in the size of the coefficients and standard errors occur. For example, students
from countries with a higher GDP p.c. have a higher probability of intending
to leave (p < 0.05), an effect that was already marginally significant in Tab. 1,


8 Results of the robustness checks are available upon request.


164 T. Thies


model 2. Further, originating from a more secure home country is positively related to intentions to leave (p < 0.05), but this association loses significance when
individual characteristics are included.
Third, it was assumed that characteristics of the home country affect students’ intentions to leave Germany. However, when students indicate that they
want to leave Germany after graduation, they may not necessarily think of going
back to their home country. Especially highly qualified migrants are characterized by transnational behavior, meaning that they are used to living, studying, and
working in different countries and have transnational networks (Schittenhelm &
Schäfer, 2019). The effect of the higher-level country characteristics may have
been non-significant (p > 0.05) because students are planning to go to some
other country rather than their country of origin. Future research could evaluate
intentions to stay for graduates in the International Student Survey. In the survey,
graduates answered a question on return migration that distinguished Germany,
the country of origin, and some other country. The present study focused on
students at the beginning of their studies because, at this point, panel attrition
problems are less severe.
The last limitation is that the dependent variable was collected retrospectively
in wave 2, while many independent variables were collected in wave 1. There is
no information on the dependent variable for students who did not participate in
the second wave. Further, the answers may not exactly reflect the intentions to
stay at the beginning of the first semester but may reflect intentions to stay in the
first year in general.


**8** **Conclusion and discussion**


In the light of an increasing shortage of skilled labor in Europe, this paper addresses the question whether international students from third countries differ from
students of EU/EFTA countries in their intentions to leave Germany after graduation. International students from EU/EFTA countries enjoy freedom of movement
in Germany. They do not need a residence permit and benefit from free access
to the labor market. On the other hand, students from third countries are faced
with residence regulations that facilitate labor market integration in Germany
immediately after graduation if they wish to stay. Further, they originate more
often from countries with lower economic development and lower political and
societal security compared to Germany. Therefore, it was expected that students
from third countries have lower intentions to leave Germany and are more certain
about their migration intentions after graduation.


International students in higher education … 165


The results suggest that more than half of the international students in the
sample plan to stay in Germany after graduation. These findings are in line with
earlier German studies (Koenings et al., 2020; Lokhande, 2017). Contrary to
expectations, students from third countries do not have lower intentions to leave
Germany. In line with expectations, students from third countries indicate less
often that they do not know whether to leave or stay in Germany. This effect is
not explained by the economic development and the political and societal security of the home countries. Further, it is not explained by students’ characteristics
and financial or security considerations when migrating to Germany. It seems
plausible that the German immigration regulations regarding third-country nationals are the reason why students from third countries are more certain about
their intentions to stay or leave at the beginning of their studies. If they plan to
stay, they have a limited time of 18 months to find a job within their field of
study. Therefore, they often start applying even before finishing their degree and
hence should be certain about their intentions earlier on. Further, they may have
migrated because gaining an education in the host country is seen as a possibility
to integrate and settle in a country with high skill returns (Bijwaard & Wang,
2016; Rosenzweig, 2006).
The home country’s economic situation (measured by GDP p.c.) is not significantly associated with either intentions to leave Germany or uncertainty about
leaving or staying. GDP p.c. growth does not affect the intention to leave but
negatively affects the probability of being still uncertain about future migration
plans. Moreover, the home country’s political and societal security (measured by
the Global Peace Index) does not significantly affect intentions to leave. This contradicts previous studies in the United States using administrative data (Bratsberg,
1995; Rosenzweig, 2006) as well as a German survey study (Koenings et al.,
2020). A reason for the differences in results could be that characteristics of
the international student population (e.g., countries of origin, financial resources,
fields of study) differ between countries and between universities within a country. Moreover, samples might differ: While this study includes international
master’s students with a bachelor’s degree gained in Germany, Koenings et al.
(2020) exclude this group from the analyses.
The present study found that especially individual considerations impact intentions to leave the host country. This is in line with Wolpert (1965), who states
that individual perceptions of the origin and destination points are important for
the decision to move. However, it is not the hope for an improvement in financial living conditions that increases intentions to stay, but rather the wish for an
improvement in security-related living conditions. Having migrated to Germany
in order to live in safety is associated with lower intentions to leave and lower


166 T. Thies


uncertainty about future migration plans. The fact that considerations of security
are important for intentions to leave needs to be interpreted in the temporal context of the data collection. The first questionnaire was distributed to students who
started university in the winter semester of 2017/2018. In 2015 and 2016, Germany experienced a high increase in the number of positive first asylum decisions
(BAMF, 2020a). Hence it could be that a number of international students in the
sample have a residence permit for humanitarian, legal, or political reasons. [9] As
the survey did not include a question on the type of residence permit, it is unclear
how many refugees are included in the sample and whether the purpose of residence affects their intentions to leave. However, this would be a fruitful question
for further research.
With respect to individual-level characteristics, this study shows that, as in previous studies (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Koenings et al., 2020; Lokhande, 2017),
having a partner and the location of the partner is crucial for return migration intentions. As in the German study by Koenings et al. (2020), social ties with German
studentsarelessimportant,whileapreviousresidenceinthehostcountryisnegatively
associated with intentions of return migration. Older students have lower intentions
to leave after graduation and are less often uncertain about their decision, which is
in line with studies on international students’ (Bijwaard & Wang, 2016; Koenings
et al., 2020; Lu et al., 2009) and immigrants’ (Constant & Massey, 2002; Diehl &
Preisendörfer, 2007) migration intentions as well as theoretical expectations (Kley,
2009). In contrast to previous studies, master’s students do not differ from bachelor’s students in their intentions to leave (Koenings et al., 2020; Lokhande, 2017). In
agreementwithearlierresearch(Koeningsetal., 2020),engineeringstudentsexpress
lower intentions to leave compared to students in a non-STEM field. The favorable
labor market situation for professionals in technology and engineering-related fields
in Germany could explain the findings.
This study finds that German language skills are associated with lower intentions to leave and a lower probability of being uncertain about leaving. Further
research could examine whether increases in language skills cause a decrease in
intentions to leave and the probability of leaving. Proficiency in the German language is often asked for by employers and is a necessity for daily life in Germany.
Low language skills are a barrier to labor market access for international students.
Free language courses for international students during their studies or help with
job applications could facilitate both the decision to stay in Germany and transition to the labor market. A study for Germany shows that helping international


9 In Germany, refugees are allowed to study at a university.


International students in higher education … 167


students with job applications increases their probability of staying in Germany
(Lokhande, 2017).
While this study aimed to identify factors that affect international students’
intentions to leave, policymakers need to decide about the goals of the internationalization strategy of higher education. Attracting and keeping international
students may be desirable for Germany and other European countries in order to
counter the shortage of skilled labor. However, the education of international students who plan to return to their home countries may be beneficial to developing
countries and counteract “brain drain” phenomena (Rech, 2012).
